Global Demand for Gold Falls --- A 37% Tumble in China Acted as a Drag in Third Quarter; 'It Is the New Normal'
Commodities Report
Global Demand for Gold Falls --- A 37% Tumble in China Acted as a Drag in Third Quarter; 'It Is the
New Normal'
By Biman Mukherji
826 words
14 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
China's demand for gold plunged in the third quarter, the latest setback for a market already suffering from
waning investor appetite.
The 37% slide in China acted as a drag on global demand, which fell 2% from the year-earlier period to 929.3
tons, industry group World Gold Council said in a report released Thursday.
The price of gold is 39% below the record hit in August 2011, with the latest leg of the selloff tied to
expectations that the U.S. economy will continue to pick up speed in coming months. Many money managers
believe that could prompt the Federal Reserve to raise short-term interest rates next year. Higher rates are
likely to further dim the allure of the precious metal, which yields nothing and costs money to hold.
Expectations for higher rates in the U.S. also weigh on gold in another way. Investors anticipating a rate
increase have snapped up dollar-denominated assets, causing the dollar to strengthen against major
currencies. That has lessened the need for gold as protection against a declining dollar, one of the reasons
U.S. investors piled into gold after the financial crisis.
Some fund managers had looked to Chinese demand for gold jewelry and bullion as a potential support for
prices. The world's second-largest economy liberalized gold ownership in 2002, and gold has become a
popular gift for weddings and during the Lunar New Year.
But that support has failed to materialize. A buying binge last year, when an abrupt drop in prices spurred
Chinese consumers to snap up gold, has proved short-lived.
In the year ended Sept. 30, Chinese gold demand has declined 27%, according to the council's report.
The industry group in April projected that Chinese gold demand would be flat this year.
"After extraordinary growth, we have to go back to a steady market," said Albert Cheng, managing director for
the Far East for the World Gold Council. "I would say it is the new normal."
Subdued consumer prices are a factor behind gold's flagging popularity with Chinese buyers, said Jack Ablin,
chief investment officer at BMO Private Bank, which has $68 billion under management.
In recent years, some investors flocked to gold to protect against inflation amid worries that extraordinary
stimulus programs by the world's biggest central banks would stoke a rise in consumer prices. But the pace of
consumer-price growth in China is near five-year lows.
"There really isn't much reason to protect yourself from inflation, because there isn't much inflation out
there," Mr. Ablin said. "As a store of value, gold is really not needed."
Mr. Ablin has avoided gold over the past several years, believing prices for the metal have more room to fall
as the Fed moves toward raising interest rates in 2015.
Still, some investors have sought gold as a haven this year after geopolitical flare-ups in Ukraine and the
Middle East. That has helped stabilize gold prices, which are down 3.4% this year so far.
The gold contract for November delivery settled 0.2% higher at $1,161.10 a troy ounce on Thursday on the
Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In India, gold demand surged in the third quarter, as sliding prices of gold in August and September spurred
jewelry buying ahead of the festivals of Dhanteras and Diwali, occasions many Hindus consider auspicious to
buy the metal.
Indian jewelry demand rose 60% to 182.9 tons during the quarter from the year-earlier period, when the
government imposed import curbs that drove up local prices. Jewelry demand in India also was propped up
by the removal of some import restrictions on the metal earlier this year.
Combined, India and China account for half of the world's gold demand.
U.S. consumers also bought more gold jewelry as many were more confident of an economic recovery,
according to the World Gold Council.
Overall investment demand globally for gold, including bars, coins and exchange-traded funds, was up 6% to
204 tons, as a selloff in exchange-traded funds slowed from the year-earlier period.
The fourth quarter may see slightly better results in China, according to the council. October's National Day
holidays could have briefly lifted demand, and the year-end holiday period also typically boosts sales, it said.
The demand for bars and coins in China is also expected to pick up heading into the Lunar New Year in
February, although a government crackdown on corruption is likely to contain any exuberance, Mr. Cheng of
the gold council said.
"It is being reflected in the gold market in terms of gift giving such as gold bars, which was reduced," he said.
---
Ira Iosebashvili and Tatyana Shumsky contributed to this article.

Dow Pauses After String of Records
Wednesday's Markets
Dow Pauses After String of Records
By Alexandra Scaggs
670 words
13 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Most U.S. stocks edged lower, breaking a five-session streak of record highs.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average slipped 2.70 points, or less than 0.1%, to 17612.20. The S&P 500 lost
1.43 points, or 0.1%, to 2038.25. The Nasdaq Composite Index, however, gained 14.58 points, or 0.3%, to
4675.13.
Stocks had been grinding slowly higher going into Wednesday's session. The market has gotten a boost from
an improved U.S. economy and better-than-forecast corporate earnings. At the same time, investors have
become more confident that the Federal Reserve will hold off on raising interest rates until the second half of
next year.
Against this backdrop, the S&P 500 has rallied 9.4% in less than a month and has fully recovered from a
sharp pullback in late September and early October. For the year, the Dow has hit 24 closing records and the
S&P has closed at 40 highs.
But with no major economic data on the calendar, and little other news affecting the broad market, traders
said investors were pausing after the latest rally.
"We've been making all-time high after all-time high, so it's important for the market to find stability along the
course of the rally," said David Lebovitz, global market strategist for J.P. Morgan Funds, which manages
$500 billion. "Sometimes that entails a modest pullback."
In Europe, however, another batch of downbeat economic news sent stock prices lower. Eurostat said
production by factories, mines and utilities during September rose a slower-than-expected 0.6% from August.
The Stoxx Europe 600 declined 1.1% and Germany's DAX lost 1.7%.
Adding to investor concerns, the Bank of England cut its forecasts for growth and inflation and signaled that
an interest-rate increase is unlikely to come until the second half of 2015. The FTSE 100 index fell 0.2%.
But in U.S. trading, company-specific news was the driver, rather than broad economic news.
Consumer-discretionary stocks advanced 0.5%, supported by gains in retailers after their earnings reports.
Fossil Group shares increased $8.73, or 8.4%, to $112.48 after per-share earnings and revenue beat
forecasts.
Macy's rose 2.98, or 5.1%, to 61.57 after per-share earnings topped analysts' expectations.
Twitter shares rallied 2.95, or 7.5%, to 42.54 as its executives spoke to Wall Street analysts about their
long-term growth strategy and unveiled plans for features such as direct video sharing and targeted
notifications.
Meanwhile, financial stocks fell as six banks -- HSBC Holdings, Royal Bank of Scotland Group, UBS,
Citigroup, J.P. Morgan Chase and Bank of America -- reached a settlement to resolve allegations that they
had worked together to try to manipulate the foreign-exchange market to boost their profits. The banks
agreed to pay a total of about $4.3 billion to U.S., British and Swiss regulators.
J.P. Morgan Chase shares fell 81 cents, or 1.3%, to 60.56, and Goldman Sachs shares fell 1.70, or 0.9%, to
190.18. The two banks were among the biggest decliners in the Dow. Bank of America slipped three cents, or
0.2%, to 17.29 and Citigroup fell 39 cents, or 0.7%, to 53.42.

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In commodities markets, oil prices continued to drop, leading to a 0.9% decline in the energy sector of the
S&P 500. Crude-oil futures fell 1% to $77.18 a barrel, their lowest settlement price since October 2011.
Gold futures slipped 0.3% to $1,158.90 an ounce.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note was unchanged at 2.359%.
Overseas early Thursday, Japan's Nikkei was up 0.3%, Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index was flat, while the
Shanghai composite, South Korea's Kospi and Australia's S&P/ASX 200 were each down by 0.2%.

Silver Stalwarts See Bargain
Silver Stalwarts See Bargain
By Tatyana Shumsky
991 words
12 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Mom-and-pop investors are loading up on silver despite a price drop that has left the metal at a four-year low.
The enduring interest can be seen in iShares Silver Trust, the largest exchange-traded fund that buys silver
bars and is a popular bet for individual investors. As cash pours in, the ETF has accumulated 345 million
ounces of silver, close to a 3 1/2-year high.
Another indicator of the metal's untarnished allure: The U.S. Mint said last Wednesday it sold out of
one-ounce American Eagle silver coins, a quintessential small-investor purchase. October saw the Mint's
strongest silver coin sales since January 2013, the last time the Mint sold out of its silver inventory.
Individual investors play a bigger role in silver than in other markets, including gold, where hedge funds and
other large money managers tend to dominate trading, analysts say. The metal appeals to buyers who
distrust financial markets and are looking for protection against future economic turmoil, such as a
stock-market crash or runaway inflation.
An ounce of gold costs about 70 times more than an ounce of silver, so even a small investment in silver can
buy a relatively large amount of the metal. Some retail investors say they view silver as a bargain after three
years of plunging prices at a time stocks and bonds are hitting new highs.
John Alfred Asuncion, a 25-year-old Filipino worker on an oil platform in the North Sea, bought 120 shares of
the iShares Silver ETF in September, his second investment ever after a losing bet on Xerox Corp. stock.
Mr. Asuncion has lost about $180 so far. But he said he prefers owning silver to stock because he sees the
metal as similar to money and therefore more likely to hold its value over the long term. He plans to keep his
ETF shares for three to five years and believes silver prices will double or triple as they rebound from recent
lows.
"Since I'm a first-time trader, I wanted to hold on to it and I don't need the money regardless how low it goes,"
Mr. Asuncion said. "This is the time to invest."
The most heavily traded silver futures contract rose 0.7 cent, or 0.04%, Tuesday to settle at $15.678 a troy
ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. This is down 68% from the record
settlement of $48.599 an ounce hit April 29, 2011.
Investors who manage at least $100 million own about 15% of shares in the iShares Silver Trust, which is
operated by BlackRock Inc., compared with about 36% of the world's largest gold ETF, according to
Morningstar Inc. Small investors, ranging from individuals with a few shares to investment advisers buying on
behalf of dozens of clients, make up much of the remainder, analysts at the data provider said.
Hedge funds and other larger investors are more tactical, moving in and out of the market frequently. For
them, wagers on precious metals were a way to benefit from low interest rates and fears of high inflation as
the Federal Reserve printed money to stimulate the economy after the 2008 financial crisis.
But starting last year many of these institutional traders fled when the central bank signaled plans to wind
down its stimulus program, which was concluded last month. The SPDR Gold Trust, a gold ETF once
popular with large hedge funds, has seen holdings drop 46% since the start of 2013. Over the same stretch,
the iShares Silver Trust's holdings rose 6.4%.
In contrast, small investors tend to take a long view on precious metals, treating them as portfolio insurance
against unexpected swings in stocks and bonds, said James Steel, a precious-metals analyst with HSBC in
New York. Many individual investors hold assets for decades until retirement and are content to wait out a
bear market.

--- Page 10 ---
Page 10 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The retail investors are clinging on and hoping that this marks the low point and they're willing to speculate
that the price will eventually go higher again," said Robin Bhar, head of metals research at Societe Generale
in London.
Hank Madden, chief executive of Madden Advisory Services, a financial adviser in Jacksonville, Fla., has
been steadily adding to his firm's iShares Silver holdings throughout 2014. Mr. Madden said he bought shares
because commodities such as silver are a hedge against unexpected crises.
"We tell our clients: You want to see yourself taking a shellacking in metals, because that means the rest of
the portfolio is performing well," said Mr. Madden, who manages $450 million and keeps about 1% of that in
silver.
Investors are expected to accumulate as much as one billion ounces of silver through financial instruments
over the next decade, in addition to the 862 million ounces they purchased between 2006 and 2013, said
CPM Group Inc., a New York-based metals consultancy.
Not everyone is willing to wait for silver prices to recover. Clara Basile, portfolio manager at Avalon Capital
Management in Redwood City, Calif., with $225 million under management, had purchased iShares Silver
shares at the start of 2014. As silver posted steep losses in September, Ms. Basile sold the shares.
"I'm not going to sit there when something is melting down," she said. Ms. Basile now predicts silver could
sink as low as $8 an ounce.
But individual investors like Charlie Fitzgerald IV, a recent business-school graduate in Winter Park, Fla., say
they see value where others see trash. Mr. Fitzgerald, 24, first became interested in silver in 2010, when his
grandmother gave him some silver coins for his birthday. Over the past three months, he has been buying up
iShares Silver Trust shares even as prices moved lower.
"Silver is currency; it's been money since the Pharaohs ruled Egypt," he said.

Stocks Edge Up to Another Record --- Home-Builder Shares Provide One Pocket of Activity, Getting Boost From D.R. Horton Order Data
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Edge Up to Another Record --- Home-Builder Shares Provide One Pocket of Activity, Getting
Boost From D.R. Horton Order Data
By Alexandra Scaggs
583 words
12 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks crept higher in the final minutes of trading Tuesday to close at a fifth-straight record high for the
Dow Jones industrials and S&P 500.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 1.16 points, less than 0.1%, to 17614.90. The S&P 500 gained 1.42
points, or 0.1%, to 2039.68, and the Nasdaq Composite Index tacked on 8.94 points, or 0.2%, to 4660.56.
U.S. stocks have pushed higher since the middle of October, recovering from a several-week swoon that
began in mid-September.
Fueling buying sentiment are data showing a healthier U.S. economy, better-than-expected third-quarter
corporate profits and expectations that the Federal Reserve won't begin raising interest rates until the
second half of 2015.
With the help of Tuesday's small gains, the Dow is up 6.3% so far this year, and the S&P 500 has advanced
10%. The Nasdaq is up 12% for 2014.
But the bounce in stocks has slowed in recent sessions after wild swings in the market in October.
Last month, the S&P moved by 1% or more in either direction in 12 sessions, and the Dow saw 13 such
swings. Neither has posted a one-day move of 1% or more in November.
With no major news Tuesday to drive the broad market, stocks drifted throughout the session, which saw the
lowest trading volume since late September. Adding to the quiet day, U.S. bond markets were closed for
Veterans Day.
"It's not unusual to get these lackluster days when you're trading at all-time highs," said Keith Bliss, senior
vice president at brokerage Cuttone & Co. Bullish investors "don't really have to do much. And the guys who
have been saying this market needs to fall have gotten burned."
One pocket of activity was in home-builder stocks. D.R. Horton Inc. gained 52 cents, or 2.2%, to $23.95 after
it reported higher orders and home-sale closings across most of the country, though its earnings fell short of
Wall Street's forecasts. The $1.6 billion iShares U.S. Home Construction exchange-traded fund advanced 43
cents, or 1.7%, to $25.22.
The rise in home builders supported gains in the consumer-discretionary sector of the S&P 500, which gained
0.4% during a busy week for retailers' earnings.
The dollar hit a seven-year high against the Japanese yen Tuesday, breaking though 116 yen for the first
time since October 2007. Late in the day, the dollar traded at 115.80 yen.
The Stoxx Europe 600 gained 0.4%.
In Asia early Wednesday, stocks in Japan extended gains with the yen continuing to trade near a seven-year
low, while in Taiwan, shares edged lower on expectations that a China-South Korea free-trade agreement
could pressure Taiwanese exporters. The Nikkei Stock Average was up 1.3%, adding to a 2.1% gain
Tuesday. Taiwan's Taiex was down 0.7%, South Korea's Kospi was up 0.5% and Hong Kong's Hang Seng
index was up 0.2%. Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was down 0.8%.
In commodity markets Tuesday, crude-oil futures gained 0.7% to $77.94 a barrel. Gold futures gained 0.3%
to $1,162.80 a troy ounce.

Dow, S&P 500 Reach Four Straight Records --- Oil-Sensitive Stocks Sink; 'Mood Is That Things Are OK'
Monday's Markets
Dow, S&P 500 Reach Four Straight Records --- Oil-Sensitive Stocks Sink; 'Mood Is That Things Are
OK'
By Dan Strumpf
780 words
11 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Dow industrials and the S&P 500 carved out their fourth straight record highs, extending a recent rally
driven by signs of an improving U.S. economy and better-than-expected corporate profits.

U.S. employers added jobs at a moderate pace in October.

for stocks. While revenue growth has been slow, profits at companies in the S&P 500 are on track to post
earnings growth of 8% in the third quarter, according to FactSet. Analysts had been expecting a 4.5%
increase in profits.

increases, which are expected in the second half of next year.
The recent rally in stocks has reversed last month's slide, with the Dow up 9.3% from its Oct. 16 low. The
S&P 500 has gained 9.4% since its recent nadir on Oct. 15.
Many investors say the slowing economy in Europe and flagging growth in China make staying invested in
U.S. companies the safest bet.

Precious Metals Slide, as Dollar Takes a Bite
By Ira Iosebashvili
519 words
6 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold and silver prices sank to their lowest point in more than 4 1/2 years, weighed down by a soaring dollar
and expectations of more stimulus from the world's largest economies.
Gold for November delivery, the front-month contract, fell $22, or 1.9%, to $1,145.40 a troy ounce on
Wednesday, its lowest settlement level since April 2010.
Front-month silver dropped 50.9 cents, or 3.2%, to $15.4180 an ounce, its lowest level since February 2010.
Bank of Japan Gov. Haruhiko Kuroda defended last week's unexpected increase in asset purchases and
pledged to do whatever it takes to eliminate deflation and spark growth, putting the central bank on a
different path from the U.S., which ended asset purchases in October and is expected to raise interest rates
next year.
The news sent the dollar higher against the yen and other currencies, as investors often equate monetary
easing with a weaker currency, in part because lower rates make a currency less attractive to hold. Precious
metals are priced in dollars and become more expensive to foreign buyers when the U.S. currency climbs.
Gold investors also kept an eye on the European Central Bank, which concludes its monetary-policy
meeting on Thursday. Some investors expect the ECB to eventually increase its stimulus measures, a move
that could strengthen the dollar against the euro and lead to more weakness in gold.
On Friday, the Labor Department will report nonfarm payrolls. A strong U.S. jobs report likely would lead
investors to believe the Fed will raise rates sooner than expected, also benefiting the dollar.
The strengthening dollar and more hawkish Federal Reserve are undercutting expectations for rising
inflation. Investors typically seek the metal as a hedge against the effects of inflation.
"Despite the trillions of dollars of stimulus over the past several years, most central bankers are worried about
deflation, not inflation," said Edward Meir, a strategist at INTL FCStone. "In addition, the roaring U.S. equity
markets continue to siphon off assets away from alternative investments, including gold."
Silver, which is used for industrial applications, is hurt by slowing growth in China, Japan and Europe, Mr.
Meir said.
Gold prices have retreated 17% from their highest point this year in March. Some investors believe the
metal's slide may halt when it declines to $1,050 to $1,080 an ounce, the estimated cost for companies to
produce an ounce of gold. At that level, producers may mine less of the metal, shrinking global supplies, said
Bob Haberkorn, a broker at RJO Futures. "Until then, I wouldn't touch it," Mr. Haberkorn said.
Platinum and palladium joined in the rout as well, with investors worried that demand for the two metals,
which are used primarily in auto exhaust filters, would suffer as the global economy slows. Palladium fell
4.2%, to $757.85 an ounce, its biggest one-day drop since June 12. Platinum settled down 1.2%, at
$1,210.60 an ounce, its lowest level since July 2009.

Yen's Fall Adds to Bears' Winnings --- Japanese Central Bank's Move to Further Ease Monetary Policy Is Boon to Discovery Capital, Soros
Currency Trading
Yen's Fall Adds to Bears' Winnings --- Japanese Central Bank's Move to Further Ease Monetary Policy
Is Boon to Discovery Capital, Soros
By James Ramage and Gregory Zuckerman
945 words
4 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The yen slumped anew to its lowest level against the dollar since 2007, handing fresh profits to investors who
had bet against the currency but sending shivers through Asian financial markets.
For much of the past year, wagering that the yen would fall while Japanese stocks would rise has been
among the most popular trades for hedge funds. Many of these investors believe that the nation's central
bank and government are committed to a lower yen to halt deflation and recharge the economy by making
exports cheaper.
Some funds had exited from bearish yen bets as recently as a few weeks ago, forced to pare back due to
increased volatility across financial markets and poor performance in many cases. Those that remained in the
trade benefited from Friday's surprise move by the Japanese central bank to further loosen monetary policy.
Discovery Capital Management LLC, which has been buffeted by losses this year, had maintained a bet that
the yen would weaken against the dollar going into Friday, said a person familiar with the matter.
Eric Mindich's $9 billion Eton Park Capital Management LP had poured into Japanese stocks and options in
recent months while shorting the yen, according to investor communications and a person familiar with the
firm.
Another winner: George Soros's $25 billion Soros Fund Management LLC, people close to the matter said.
The firm has made hundreds of millions of dollars in profit since the Friday announcement, a person said.
"It's one of the most widely held, crowded positions in all of hedge funds," said Adam Taback, president of
alternative strategies at Wells Fargo Private Bank, which manages about $10 billion in hedge-fund
investments.
Some investors say they are holding firm on their bearish bets on the yen, anticipating the central bank's
actions will further weaken the currency by diminishing the appeal of Japanese debt. They also are looking
beyond Japan. Shares in South Korean auto makers and other exporters in that country dived on concerns
that the falling yen would hand an advantage to Japanese companies. Gold prices slipped to a four-year low
as a stronger dollar damped the metal's appeal to investors.
The yen's sharp move since Friday is the latest example of how a widening gap between growth prospects in
the U.S. and other major economies is playing out in financial markets. U.S. stocks and the dollar have
soared as the economy improves, bringing the Federal Reserve closer to raising interest rates. Meantime,
economies in Japan and much of Europe appear to be backsliding, forcing central bankers to adopt new
stimulus measures.
"We've been expecting that . . . the BOJ would have to do more, because growth and inflation continue to
come in below target," said Scott Mather, chief investment officer of U.S. core strategy at Pacific Investment
Management Co., which manages $1.87 trillion. "They just acted sooner than most expected."
Pimco, a unit of Allianz SE, is maintaining its bearish yen bets in anticipation of further declines in the
currency, Mr. Mather said, adding that he expects the pace of weakening to slow.
On Monday, the yen breached 114 to the dollar, its worst two-day loss since April 2013, when the BOJ first
unveiled its asset-purchase plan. Stocks surged in Tokyo early Tuesday with the Nikkei Stock Average up
3.8%. In late New York trading, one dollar bought 114.05 yen, up from 112.31 yen Friday and 109.22 yen
Thursday, before the BOJs announcement.

--- Page 22 ---
Page 22 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The BOJ on Friday said it would increase its annual asset purchases in its effort to boost inflation toward
2%. As the BOJ prints money to buy bonds, it should drive down their yields. Investors seeking better returns
would then move money overseas, which could renew pressure on the yen, Mr. Mather said.
Foreign investors already had driven the yen lower by betting against the currency in the foreign-exchange
market and by selling Japanese government bonds. However, it is likely to be Japanese investors who
weaken their currency going forward, investors and analysts say.
On Friday, Japan's Government Pension Investment Fund, the world's largest public pension fund, said it
planned to sell government bonds and buy stocks. While some of that money will head into Japanese
equities, the rest will be moved abroad. "This signals a significant outflow of yen into foreign assets, a
negative for the currency," said Vassili Serebriakov, currency strategist at BNP Paribas. The bank predicts
the yen will fall to 115 per dollar in the first three months of next year and 124 by the end of 2015.
Investor net bets against the yen were at a high for the year in the futures market at the end of September but
had fallen since then, according to the Commodity Futures Trading Commission. Currency futures are a
comparatively small part of the foreign-exchange market but often reflect wider trends.
Most hedge funds have performed poorly lately, including so-called macro funds, or those that bet on global
financial markets. Many funds that at one point had large Japanese positions either cut or eliminated them
over the past few months.
The prospect of lower yields in Japan also is making it more appealing to borrow yen to buy other currencies
that offer better returns, said Robert Tipp, chief investment strategist at Prudential Fixed Income, which
manages $533 billion.

Gold Prices Drop 2.3% to a Four-Year Low --- Surging Dollar After Japan's Surprise Stimulus Plans Makes the Precious Metal More Expensive...
Commodities Report
Gold Prices Drop 2.3% to a Four-Year Low --- Surging Dollar After Japan's Surprise Stimulus Plans
Makes the Precious Metal More Expensive for Some Purchasers
By Tatyana Shumsky
632 words
1 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold slumped to a four-year low on Friday, after Japan's plans to boost its stimulus efforts catapulted the
dollar near a seven-year high against the yen and fueled a rally in U.S. stocks.
The Bank of Japan unexpectedly announced it would expand its bond purchases to 80 trillion yen ($732.47
billion) from the previous target range of 60 trillion yen to 70 trillion yen. The central bank hopes to spur
business activity and inflation by purchasing more Japanese government bonds.
In response, gold prices plunged, while the dollar rose to a two-year high against the euro. Gold is priced in
dollars, so a stronger greenback makes the metal more expensive for buyers who use other currencies,
limiting demand.
"The gold market was already vulnerable, and the last thing it needed was this type of news," said Bill O'Neill,
a principal with Logic Advisors LLC, a commodity investment firm.
Gold has come under renewed pressure in recent months, as investors bought the dollar on expectations of
tighter monetary policy in the U.S. The Federal Reserve on Wednesday said it would end its stimulus
program after October and remained on track to raise interest rates next year. The Fed's shifting stance is
undercutting one of the most common rationales for holding gold: that lax monetary policies would weaken
the dollar and lead to rampant inflation.
Friday's move by the BOJ also highlights the expanding gap between U.S. monetary policy, which is shifting
to reining in liquidity, and that of Japan, where the central bank is committed to asset purchases and other
easy-money tools. As a result, global investors are more likely to funnel their money toward the U.S. to
benefit from higher interest rates. This would further boost the dollar and dull gold's allure.
Gold for December delivery, the most actively traded contract, settled down $27, or 2.3%, at $1,171.60 a troy
ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. This was the lowest settlement price
since July 29, 2010. November gold tumbled $27, or 2.3%, to $1,1171.10 an ounce.
"Any news that reinforces the divergence in monetary policies will be a positive for the dollar and immediately
negative for gold," said Paul Christopher, chief international strategist at Wells Fargo Advisors, which has
$1.3 trillion in assets.
U.S. stocks are also drawing investors away from gold. Two major U.S. share indexes advanced to records
on Friday. While some investors view gold as a store of value, a brighter economic outlook boosts the
attraction of riskier bets such as stocks that would benefit from a pickup in global growth.
"When equities are going up and making new highs on the year, I don't see why anyone should buy gold,"
said Adam Klopfenstein, a senior market strategist with Archer Financial Services in Chicago.
The BOJ's aggressive policy shift also highlights global concerns about low inflation. Eurozone consumer
prices rose 0.4% in October from a year earlier, a slight pickup on the 0.3% inflation reported in September,
data released Friday show.
"The writing has been there on the wall, and gold has been steadily eroding," said Mr. O'Neill.
Gold doesn't earn interest or dividends and has a harder time competing for investors' funds with
interest-bearing assets such as Treasury bonds when rates are rising.
Separately, sales of American Eagle silver coins in October hit the highest volume in almost two years, data
from the U.S. Mint showed. Sales of gold coins also climbed.

Japan's Bold Stimulus Jolts Markets --- Dow Hits A Record, Bonds Fall In U.S.
Japan's Bold Stimulus Jolts Markets --- Dow Hits A Record, Bonds Fall In U.S.
By Tom Lauricella and Corrie Driebusch
1,162 words
1 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Japan's stimulus plans rippled across global markets, pushing the Dow Jones Industrial Average to a record,
sending the dollar near a seven-year high against the Japanese yen and pulling U.S. government bond prices
lower.
The Bank of Japan's move on Friday is the latest shift in an investment landscape already characterized by
low interest rates and an investor thirst for higher-yielding assets.
By boosting annual asset purchases by as much as a third, to 80 trillion yen ($732 billion), the Bank of Japan
stands to bolster global demand for stocks by driving down bond yields, making shares relatively more
attractive. The European Central Bank in October kicked off its own round of bond buying in a bid to jolt low
inflation on the Continent, while the Federal Reserve on Wednesday ended its bond-buying program amid
steady improvement in job growth.
The expanding gap between the U.S. and European and Japanese central-bank policies has sent money
flooding into the U.S. in anticipation of higher returns.
"The world has grown addicted to easing," said Savita Subramanian, chief U.S. stock strategist at Bank of
America Merrill Lynch. In a further boost, the Japanese government's pension fund said Friday that it would
raise its allocation in Japanese and foreign stocks.
To be sure, the Bank of Japan's action reminded investors of the stiff headwinds facing the world's major
economies and underscored the sense among many analysts that asset-price rises in recent years are built
on a fragile foundation of central-bank support. The Dow industrials have risen 166% from their March 2009
financial-crisis low.
On Friday, Japan's Nikkei Stock Average jumped 4.8% to its largest point gain in six years. The surge in
Tokyo carried over to Europe, as the Stoxx Europe 600 climbed 1.8%. In the U.S., the Dow rose 195.10
points, or 1.1%, to its 19th record close this year.
Friday's trading capped a wild month that began with U.S. and European share prices falling sharply, and
yields on 10-year U.S. government bonds plunging below 2%, amid worries about the pace of global
economic growth and stubbornly low inflation.
The Dow industrials rose 2% for the month after earlier being down as much as 5%. On Friday, the blue-chip
index closed at 17390.52. The S&P 500 also hit a record, rising 23.40 points, or 1.2%, to 2018.05.
The impact of central-bank policy can be seen most clearly in currency markets. The dollar has surged in the
second half of 2014 against the euro and yen, as investors have stepped up bets that the Fed will soon raise
short-term interest rates for the first time since 2006.
On Friday, the dollar climbed 2.9% against the yen. A weaker yen could undergird a halting recovery in
Japan, as it makes exports more competitive overseas. Japan has been long beset by weak growth and a
damaging cycle of falling prices and lower spending known as deflation.
Though recent weeks have brought solid corporate earnings and upbeat U.S. economic data, concerns about
growth and low inflation haven't abated.
Gold, seen as a haven, tumbled 2.3% on Friday to a four-year low. Oil prices slid 0.7%, to $80.54 a barrel,
extending a decline that has reduced crude prices by a quarter since the spring. Both figures point to
expectations of soft global growth, traders said.

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Central banks don't embark on quantitative easing without a reason," said James Macey, a portfolio
manager on the multiasset team at Allianz Global Investors, which oversees $511 billion. Investors fear
"Japan may not be successful in breaking out" of its slump.
Bond prices eased as investors shifted out of holdings perceived as safe. The yield on the 10-year Treasury
note rose to 2.335%, from 2.305% on Thursday, leaving it well below the 3% level at which it started the year.
Bond yields move inversely to prices.
While easy-money policies have helped lift prices on financial assets such as stocks and bonds, thus far
there has been little evidence of stepped-up consumer and business spending in economies hamstrung by
government debt and aging populations.
Japan has been stuck in low growth for more than a decade, and European policy makers have warned this
year about the prospect that deflation could take hold amid signs of a fresh recession on the Continent.
The dollar has risen 46% against the yen since the start of 2012, when Japan began adopting policies aimed
at reviving growth and boosting inflation.
"Investors have gotten used to central banks taking these actions and providing support to markets, so, in the
short term, markets are pricing such actions as removing risk," said Erik Knutzen, multiasset-class chief
investment officer at Neuberger Berman. Whether easing actually helps fuel economic recovery remains to
be seen, he said.
The U.S. economy has been a relative bright spot. Economic output expanded at a 3.5% annualized clip in
the third quarter, and the jobless rate has fallen to 5.9%. U.S. corporate earnings have also been reasonably
healthy. The S&P 500 is on pace to see third-quarter earnings rise 7.5% from a year ago, according to
FactSet, above the 4.5% expected before the start of earnings season.
Even so, Friday brought fresh evidence that the U.S., too, is struggling with low inflation. The price index for
personal-consumption expenditures, the Fed's favored way to measure inflation, rose 1.4% in September, its
29th consecutive month below the central bank's 2% inflation target.
"The significant thing here is that there's no real organic growth in the developed world," said Matthew Alexy,
a director for global foreign exchange at TD Securities. "The feedback into the real economy hasn't
materialized."
Many investors have projected that U.S. bond yields would rise as growth picked up in the U.S. and
elsewhere. But bond prices have unexpectedly rallied this year amid signs that global growth is slowing and
inflation fading, particularly in Europe.
With U.S. inflation low and economic growth uneven, many investors believe it will be well into the second
half of 2015 before the Fed raises interest rates.
Jurrien Timmer, director of global macro at Fidelity Investments, said that while economic growth may be too
slow by most standards, "it's also slow enough for inflation to not be a problem, and for stocks that's pretty
good."
And despite fears of a global slowdown or a brush with deflation, the central bank's easy-money policy
generally remains "a plus for investors," he said.

Banks Face New Rules On Funding
Banks Face New Rules On Funding
By Viktoria Dendrinou
350 words
1 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BRUSSELS -- Banks will have to hold enough cash, or assets that can reliably be converted into cash, to
cover their expected outflows over a one-year horizon, global banking regulators said Friday.
The rules on the so-called Net Stable Funding Ratio, which were published Friday by the Basel Committee on
Banking Supervision, the Switzerland-based group that sets global banking standards, aim to make lenders
more resilient by keeping them from relying too heavily on short-term borrowing to fund long-term loans.
The NSFR, which will kick in at the start of 2018, requires banks to fund their activities with sufficiently stable
sources to mitigate the risk of future stress.
It forces banks to fund long-term loans from sources that won't dry up in a stress scenario, such as
high-quality capital and term deposits.
The aim is to secure so-called stable funding for over one year to cover an extended stress scenario.
"A key lesson from the crisis has been the need to prevent overreliance on short-term, volatile sources of
funding," said Stefan Ingves, chairman of the Basel Committee and governor of the Swedish central bank.
"The NSFR does this by limiting the use of volatile short-term borrowings to fund illiquid assets."
These rules will complete the committee's overhaul agenda aimed at building a more resilient financial sector
that was drawn up after the 2008 financial crisis, Mr. Ingves said.
Under the NSFR, bank assets such as gold, mortgages and other loans are assigned different weightings to
determine the extent to which they must be backed by stable funding sources.
Regulators developed the NSFR alongside another short-term measure, the liquidity-coverage ratio, to
ensure that banks have enough liquid assets to withstand a shock loss of access to funding markets, such as
the one that followed the collapse of Lehman Brothers in 2008.
The new rule broadly follows a draft consultation document published by the Basel Committee in January but
includes changes in the treatment of some loan and derivative exposures.

Fed Closes Chapter on Easy Money --- Benefit of Bond-Buying Experiment Remains Unclear as Central Bank's Focus Returns to Interest Rates
Fed Closes Chapter on Easy Money --- Benefit of Bond-Buying Experiment Remains Unclear as
Central Bank's Focus Returns to Interest Rates
By Jon Hilsenrath
1,107 words
30 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Federal Reserve said it would end its long-running bond-purchase program, concluding a historic
experiment that stirred disagreement among policy makers, economists and investors about its impact even
though the central bank said it helped accomplish its goal of reducing unemployment.
The move and the Fed's accompanying assessment of current conditions were a vote of confidence in the
U.S. economy, which many economists peg to have grown at an annual pace near 3% or more in the third
quarter. That's a much better performance than in Japan or Europe and a hopeful sign for the world economy
when growth in China appears to be flagging.
"There has been a substantial improvement in the outlook for the labor market since the inception of [the]
current asset-purchase program," the Fed said in its policy statement, released Wednesday after a two-day
policy meeting. "Moreover, the [Fed] continues to see sufficient underlying strength in the broader economy
to support ongoing progress toward maximum employment in a context of price stability."
The program ends with mixed reviews. While it clearly didn't cause the inflation outbreak some predicted, it
also didn't clearly lead to a surge of economic output or hiring.
If all goes as they expect, officials will now turn their attention in the months ahead to discussions about when
to start raising interest rates and how to signal those moves to the public before they happen. For now the
central bank stuck to an assurance that it will keep short-term interest rates near zero for a "considerable
time." Many investors and Fed officials expect no rate increases until the middle of next year.
Plenty could go wrong and drive the Fed to tear up its plans. Twice before officials declared they would stop
printing new money to buy bonds, only to restart the process when growth, hiring and inflation appeared to
sag. One official, Minneapolis Fed President Narayana Kocherlakota, dissented from the Fed statement
Wednesday because he wanted to keep the bond program going.
Underscoring its own uncertainty, the Fed's official rate assurance included a new qualifier about the
economic outlook: If the job market improves more quickly than expected or inflation rises, rate hikes could
come sooner, and it could wait longer if the job market or inflation slow.
The inflation and employment backdrop now is subtly pulling the central bank in two directions. Officials
noted "solid job gains" and said labor-market slack is "gradually diminishing," signs of economic vigor that
might spur early rate increases. Since July the Fed had been saying it saw "significant" slack, a phrase it
struck this time around.
But Fed officials also noted falling energy prices and downward pressure on market expectations of inflation,
signs of low inflation that give them leeway to wait before raising rates. Inflation has been running below the
Fed's 2% target for more than two years.
While Fed officials consider their next steps, economists and market participants are writing postmortems on
whether the bond program worked.
Debate of late has gotten heated. In exchanges online earlier this month, Paul Krugman, a Nobel
Prize-winning economist and columnist, accused critics of the bond program of whining and being
unreasonable and unwilling to admit mistakes assessing its risks. Cliff Asness, a hedge-fund manager and
one of those critics, likened Mr. Krugman to a skunk. Mr. Krugman shot back that Mr. Asness was accusing
him of "being a big meanie."
The bond-buying program, known as quantitative easing, is controversial in part because it involves many
unknowns. In normal times, the Fed tries to modulate the swings of economic upturns and downturns by
raising or lowering short-term interest rates. When it pushed rates to near zero in December 2008, officials

--- Page 29 ---
Page 29 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
were left scrambling to find other ways to stimulate financial markets and economic activity in response to the
downturn and they've leaned heavily on bond purchases.
The Fed -- seeking to lower interest rates and push investors into risky assets, and in turn spur borrowing,
spending, investment, growth and hiring -- launched the latest round of bond purchases in September 2012,
when it said it would buy $40 billion a month of mortgage bonds and keep going until it saw substantial
improvement in the job market. It expanded the purchases in December 2012 by saying it would also buy $45
billion a month in Treasury bonds, for a total of $85 billion monthly. It has been phasing out the program
gradually since January.
The program was pioneered by former Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke. He started pulling it back early this year
and his successor, Janet Yellen, has followed through on his plan to end it.
Critics of the Fed for years have argued the strategy risked stoking inflation, devaluation of the dollar and
market distortions.
The worst fears about bond buying haven't come to pass. Inflation, as measured by the Commerce
Department's personal consumption expenditure price index, has been unchanged at 1.5% since September
2012. Meantime, the price of gold, which some investors believe should rise when inflation fears pick up,
has fallen from $1730.60 per ounce to $1229.20, a 29% decline.
"I guess I was wrong thinking that we would recover faster than we did," Peter Wallison, co-director of the
American Enterprise Institute's financial studies program, said in an interview.
Mr. Wallison was one of several scholars, along with Mr. Asness and other market participants, who wrote an
open letter to Mr. Bernanke in 2010 warning him that he risked causing inflation and debasing the currency
with an earlier bond-buying program. Mr. Wallison said new financial regulation had choked off credit and
prevented the inflation he feared back then.
Yet clear demonstrations of QE's benefits are elusive. Job growth was 2.2 million in the 12 months before the
Fed launched the latest round of bond buying and 2.6 million in the past 12 months, but it is hard to prove the
faster growth has come even indirectly from the Fed's efforts.
"It is a mistake to think that the Fed was a major driver of either bond prices, stock prices or the economy
over the last four years," said James Hamilton, a professor at the University of California at San Diego, who
has studied the effects of bond purchases. "There has been some exaggeration of what this meant, both from
the doomsayers as well as some of the Fed's defenders."

U.S. News --- THE OUTLOOK: Market Missteps Fuel Inequality
U.S. News --- THE OUTLOOK: Market Missteps Fuel Inequality
By Josh Zumbrun
1,062 words
27 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Millions of Americans inadvertently made a classic investment mistake that contributed to today's widening
economic inequality: They bought high and sold low.
Late in the stock-market booms of the 1990s and 2000s, more U.S. families clambered into stocks as indexes
surged. Then, once markets tumbled, many households sold and took losses.
Those that held on during the most recent collapses reaped the benefits as stocks nearly tripled between
2009 and today.
The split path is one driver of stark inequality in the U.S. Many workers have seen their wealth and incomes
drop despite more than five years of economic expansion in the U.S. Some fear the gap, widening for
decades, could fracture society and slow the nation's potential for economic growth in the long run.
"One unfortunate effect of recessions and stock-market declines is they often induce people to exit the market
at exactly the wrong time," said Dean Maki, chief U.S. economist at Barclays and a former Fed researcher on
consumer balance sheets. "In retrospect, anyway, the right thing to do would have been to buy more equities
at the trough, not to sell equities at the trough."
With markets again gone wobbly after a half-decade surge in stocks, the pattern threatens to repeat. The
S&P 500 index closed down as much as 7.4% from its mid-September peak until mid-October, unsettling
some investors.
New research from the Federal Reserve and the University of Michigan shows the role that panic about the
market played in widening wealth inequality.
The Fed's Survey of Consumer Finances shows that among the bottom 90% of households by wealth,
families bailed out of the stock market between 2007 and 2010 -- the central bank's study is conducted every
three years -- and between 2010 and 2013. The total share with stockholdings declined by 4.4 percentage
points. That's the equivalent of 5.4 million households selling stocks, even as the market rebounded. Only
households in the top 10% have been increasingly likely to own stocks.
To be sure, some households that sold stocks had little choice.
The 2007-2009 recession pushed the unemployment rate to 10% just as equities declined more than 50%.
Families struggling with job loss or mortgage problems may have had no choice other than selling at a loss.
But the data suggest some investors simply sold at the wrong moment. "Even at the worst of the recession,
most people still had jobs," said Mr. Maki. "Certainly, some of the people who got out of the equity market
were doing it because of fear rather than need."
That's also the finding of new research from economists Bing Chen and Frank Stafford at the University of
Michigan. They plumbed the Panel Study of Income Dynamics, a survey that tracks the same households
over time, to evaluate the factors behind their fluctuating incomes and wealth.
Households with the highest education and strong portfolios to begin with were likely to keep buying stocks
during the decline, they found. Those with less education and smaller account balances were more likely to
sell during the downturn.
When the subsequent rebound happened, the already rich got even richer.
The finding holds even after controlling for job loss or mortgage distress, meaning some families simply sold
at the wrong time.

--- Page 33 ---
Page 33 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Stafford speculates that many households, after being burned by the market, won't have anything to do
with it. The Fed's survey suggests many of these households have bailed from traditional assets and only
increased the amount of saving that they are doing in transaction accounts.
Imagine two households, each with $100,000 in the stock market in 2007. A family that sold in 2009 after
losing half its portfolio's value may now have $50,000 in a savings account. A family that held on would now
have about $130,000 in stocks. The inequality has yawned merely because of the investing decisions.
"I have colleagues who say that these small-scale guys got burned and won't come back, and I don't think
that's the right answer," said Mr. Stafford. "Unless we get a flash of brilliance for a new pension system,
stocks are a really important ingredient."
Wealth inequality in the U.S. has many causes, some of which precede the recent booms and busts, and the
new research doesn't quantify exactly how much the stock-market timing contributed to it. The widening gap
in incomes stretches back nearly 31/2 decades. Long-term unemployment rose in the recession and has yet
to recover. And the decline in real estate hit many middle-class families that stored much of their wealth in
their homes.
In addition, many households never owned stocks to begin with. Among those with earnings in the bottom
fifth, for example, 89% don't own stocks, up from 86% in 2007.
There are signs that investors may now be returning to stocks since the latest Fed survey was conducted.
Last year was the strongest for inflows into stock mutual funds since 2004, according to the fund tracker
Lipper.
It could be the case that a stock collapse is around the corner and those who stayed in the market will lose
even more money than those who sold in 2009. Maybe this time is different and panic will be warranted.
But history suggests stocks will do well in the long run, despite their volatility, for those who hang on to the
investment. University of Pennsylvania economist Jeremy Siegel has calculated stocks have returned an
average 6.7% a year over the past two centuries, outperforming bonds, gold and the dollar.
Still, market declines continue to spook some investors into selling at huge losses.
"We haven't come up with the solution to prevent people from doing it yet," said Shai Akabas, an economist
at the Bipartisan Policy Center in Washington who works on the center's Personal Savings Initiative.
"But there certainly is a widening gap there in terms of the return that higher-income people are receiving in
the market," said Mr. Akabas. "Lower- to middle-income people aren't privy to those gains. That's
exacerbated by the fact that many of them have taken their money out of the stock market."

OFF DUTY --- Adventure & Travel: Eat Your Way Through Appalachia --- Skip the fried-squirrel jokes -- this could be the next big dining...
OFF DUTY --- Adventure & Travel: Eat Your Way Through Appalachia --- Skip the fried-squirrel jokes --
this could be the next big dining destination, and a multistate road trip is the way to experience it
By Melanie D.G. Kaplan
2,054 words
18 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
D4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
Country Girl at Heart Farm Bed & Breakfast is in Munfordville, Ky. An article in Saturday's Off Duty section
incorrectly said it is in Munford, Ky.
(WSJ October 22, 2014)
(END)
Growing up in the heart of Appalachia -- specifically, the Kentucky coal-mining town of Hazard -- my friend
Travis Fugate had little variety in his cuisine. Pinto beans, unsweetened cornbread and fried cabbage were
staples. At a young age, he knew he wanted more flavor, diversity and excitement in his meals.
He eventually found that elsewhere, but it turns out he didn't need to leave Appalachia to do so. The region,
which covers parts of 12 states between New York and Mississippi (plus all of West Virginia) isn't just a major
source of coal; it's one of the most agriculturally abundant areas in the U.S. Everything from rhubarb to ramps
grows there, and farming, canning and pickling are important aspects of the local food heritage.
The mountainous terrain that gave rise to so much bounty -- and created a distinct culture and dialect (the
"hollow" where Travis grew up sounds to me like "holler") -- has also kept the region geographically isolated
and in many ways, lagging behind nationally. Appalachia continues to rank low in terms of income,
employment, education and health. Traditional dishes reflect some of those challenges: Soup beans, made
from dried legumes and a bit of pork for flavoring, is cheap to make; stack cake is said to have originated with
friends and family contributing layers to build a wedding cake, which would otherwise have been prohibitively
expensive.
Only in recent years have chefs begun to recognize and riff on this rich heritage. And a new initiative by the
Appalachian Regional Commission, an economic development agency, aims to promote it. The Bon Appetit!
Bon Appalachia! map and website, launched this summer, spotlight hundreds of the region's most distinctive
food destinations, from farmers' markets to craft breweries to cafes that serve locally sourced berries and
beets. On the list are spots like the SustainFloyd Farmers Market in the funky town of Floyd, Va., off the Blue
Ridge Parkway; and a small, legal moonshine operation in Gilbert, a hardscrabble blip of a town in the
coalfields of West Virginia.
I had many of the same preconceptions a lot of people do about Appalachian fare (lots of fried food, the
occasional squirrel) and had never considered visiting this part of the country to eat. But the more I studied
the map, the more intrigued I was by all of the farm-to-table restaurants in off-the-tourist-track places. Travis
-- who shares my love of bluegrass music and culinary adventures -- was in Kentucky for the summer, and
agreed that a road trip was in order.
We sketched out a four-day itinerary through the heart of Appalachia -- West Virginia, Kentucky, Virginia and
North Carolina -- and chose a dozen spots to visit, a few in each town. I dreaded the hours of mountain
driving, but at least it would give our stomachs a respite between meals.
One Friday morning in August, we loaded our dogs into the car and headed west from Washington, D.C., into
the mountains. Four and a half hours later, we'd reached our first stop, Lewisburg, W.V. Just 10 miles from
the famous Greenbrier resort, the town didn't seem to suffer from the economic blight that affects much of the
region. We walked past bakeries, boutiques and art galleries, and grabbed the last open lunch table at a
decade-old bistro called Stardust Cafe.
Giant paper lanterns hung from the ceiling, and a note on the menu suggested that "slow food" might mean
leisurely delivery as well as sustainable sourcing of ingredients. We shared a locally grown green salad

--- Page 39 ---
Page 39 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
topped with plump tomatoes that tasted fresh off the vine, and a fat, juicy burger, made from locally raised,
grass-fed beef and embellished with avocado. I was glad to have made an exception for the burger in my
largely vegetarian diet.
I'd asked Jennifer "Tootie" Jones, who raised the cow that became our burger, to join us -- her Swift Level
Farm is just 4 miles away from the restaurant. After lunch, we followed her to the farm, where she raises
some 75 head of cattle and hosts cookouts and weddings. Sugar maples and oaks lined the winding
driveway, and black and brown Angus cattle dotted the hilly pastures. Showing us around, Ms. Jones said
she hoped that the new tourism initiative would help educate folks -- not just about Appalachia, but about
where their food comes from.
As we drove west on Interstate 64 toward Charleston, we crossed the New River, popular with white-water
kayakers and rafters. Travis mused that he should find a bride so they could marry at Ms. Jones's storybook
farm, but our conversation never strayed far from food. He recalled sitting on the porch with his grandmother,
breaking green beans for canning, and rushing to see what remained in his father's blackened lunch box after
a day at the mine. Many people in his hometown would be hard-pressed to even afford a meal at Stardust, he
observed.
We continued westward, and just before dinner arrived in Charleston, a riverfront city of 50,000 that combines
the splendor of a gold capitol dome with the grittiness of its industrial past. After walking the dogs around the
capitol building, we drove up the street to Bluegrass Kitchen, which specializes in high-end comfort food. The
city's East End was starkly different from Lewisburg; abandoned storefronts and empty lots stretched along
the main street. We sat in the corner of a large dining room with exposed-brick walls and a pressed tin ceiling.
And then, we over-ordered. Our table was soon covered with dishes: tomatoes stuffed with quinoa, squash,
peppers and feta; a version of Hoppin' John, the traditional Southern stew, with black-eyed peas and Swiss
chard; and a trout and grits dish with kale and bourbon-mustard-dill sauce.
The portions were better suited to people who'd spent the day doing manual labor, but we couldn't resist
finishing the meal off with blueberry buttermilk pie. Patrons at the next table offered advice on local spots.
One suggestion was Taylor Books, a high-ceilinged indie bookstore on the other side of town with a lively
coffee shop. We stopped there the next morning and flipped through hard-to-find books like "Mountain
Measures: A Collection of West Virginia Recipes" and "Folk Medicine in Southern Appalachia." At Capitol
Market, the city's indoor-outdoor farmers market, we browsed giant bins of dried beans, fresh produce,
Mason jars of jalapeno-pickled eggs and a regional relish called chow-chow, made with cabbage and onions.
As we crisscrossed the spine of the Appalachian Mountains, we found ourselves in the car for hours at a
time, but often had the road to ourselves. We listened to bluegrass and country on WVOW radio, the "Voice
of the Coalfields," and followed handwritten signs for boiled peanuts. I marveled at the kudzu that blanketed
trees and mountainsides.
Next up was Pikeville, Ky., a small mountain town that Travis said had spruced itself up since he'd last visited.
The whole population seemed to be downtown for Muscle on Main, a monthly classic-car show. The roar of
drag racing accompanied our al fresco meal at the Blue Raven, which serves "pub-style Appalachian
cuisine," like a bone-in pork chop with corn-muffin stuffing and bourbon-honey carrots. The owners grow
produce on a family farm. Travis's generously stuffed $3 short-rib biscuit appetizer turned out to be the best
value of the trip.
We spent the night in a lodge at Jenny Wiley State Resort Park, leaving early the next morning. Clouds hung
low among the mountains. The road heading southeast toward Virginia curved like a Krazy Straw, and we
listened to Sunday bluegrass gospel on the radio. "This is the kind of music we'd hear at church," Travis said.
"But at night, the lyrics are all about heartbreak and moonshinin'."
In Abingdon, in southwestern Virginia, we stopped for brunch at Heartwood, a center that showcases the
area's music, crafts and food and looks like a massive, modern interpretation of a barn and silo. Although
Heartwood sometimes caters to busloads of tourists, the food at its restaurant -- buttermilk biscuits,
heirloom-tomato-and-beet salad with goat cheese -- tasted like it could have come from a farmhouse kitchen.
Travis had gotten in the habit of asking our servers where menu ingredients were from. Some said, frankly,
that they arrived on 18-wheelers. Others named local farms. But we weren't prepared for Harvest Table, a
homey, hardwood-floored restaurant in the little town of Meadowview, Va., owned by author Barbara
Kingsolver and her husband, Steven Hopp. The server -- calling me "Honey" -- said we should try the
blueberry crisp and the carrot cake; she'd made the crisp, and the carrots came from the owners' nearby
farm.
In Boone, N.C., a hippy-outdoorsy town, we imbibed at Appalachian Brewing Company and dined at Hob Nob
Farm Cafe, a five-year-old restaurant with a surprising number of vegan options -- like a tamale with local

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
kale, portobellos, sweet potatoes and vegan creme fraiche. I opted for the real-cheese, local-vegetable
lasagna; Travis succumbed to the bacon-wrapped meatloaf.
Over the course of the trip, we'd seen coal towns struggling to reinvent themselves and restaurants straining
to be sustainable and profitable. We'd talked about the challenge of providing healthy, tasty food to
less-than-affluent locals. It seemed fitting that our last stop was Boone's F.A.R.M. (Feed All Regardless of
Means) Cafe, a pay-what-you-can kitchen where you can buy a meal, trade volunteer hours for food or pay
extra so someone else can eat later.
We sat at the counter next to a maintenance worker from Appalachian State University, who lunches there
daily. The locally sourced meal -- tomato soup, grilled cheese, green salad, potato salad, sauteed squash and
beans, and apple cobbler -- was served on a green cafeteria tray and cost just $10.
"Tomorrow would be a good day to eat here," a bearded regular offered. "Tomato Pie Tuesday, with heirloom
tomatoes and goat cheese." We later saw him sitting out front, playing the mandolin for change.
We wanted to return, and to volunteer serving meals and washing dishes. But too soon, we were on the road
again, disentangling ourselves from the embrace of the mountains, speeding along the straight lines of the
interstate, savoring memories of fresh kale and warm berry crisp.
---
The Lowdown // A Food-focused Road Trip in Appalachia
Getting There: The Appalachian region includes West Virginia and parts of 12 other states, and stretches
from southern New York to northern Mississippi. Bon Appetit stops are scattered throughout the area. Keep in
mind that routes through the mountains can be very indirect.
Eating There: The Bon Appetit! Bon Appalachia! interactive map and guide are available at
visitappalachia.com. Some highlights around the region: Bluegrass Kitchen in Charleston, W.V., serves
upscale comfort food, and cocktails with homemade garnishes, including pickled onions and ramps (1600
Washington St. E., bluegrasswv.com). In Meadowview, Va., Harvest Table sources everything from within a
150-mile radius, including seafood from the Carolina and Virginia coasts (13180 Meadowview Square,
harvesttablerestaurant.com). F.A.R.M. Cafe in Boone, N.C., is a pay-what-you-can community kitchen that
uses local ingredients (617 W. King St., farmcafe.org).
Staying There: Country Girl at Heart Farm Bed & Breakfast in Munford, Ky., is an eco-friendly inn with a
working farm (from $109 a night, bedandbreakfastkentucky.net). The property's refurbished Amish farmhouse
features skylights and organic cotton linens.
At the Inn at Evins Mill, east of Nashville in Smithville, Tenn., you can enjoy luxury and mountain views, with
creek-side accommodations and covered decks furnished with rocking chairs (from $290 a night,
evinsmill.com).

Stock Leap Caps Wild Week --- Dow Industrials Rise 263.17 Points as Buyers Step In After Four Days of Tumult

Stock Leap Caps Wild Week --- Dow Industrials Rise 263.17 Points as Buyers Step In After Four Days
of Tumult
By Alexandra Scaggs
930 words
18 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks capped their wildest week in three years on a high note Friday, with the Dow Jones Industrial
Average posting its second-largest gain of 2014.
Trading was brisk, continuing a trend seen throughout the week, as traders said a broad range of clients
stepped in to buy large-company stocks and pare back bets intended to protect against market declines, such
as earlier purchases of U.S. Treasury bonds. Some traders said the gains show the buy-the-dip mentality that
has propelled stocks since their 2009 low remains in place.
Even so, the recent surge of volatility has changed many investors' mind-set following a long period of placid
trading and multiple records on the major indexes.
"It's been a crazy week," said Doug Crofton, head of U.S. cash equity trading at Bank of America Merrill
Lynch. "It's hard to say with any conviction that this move is going to last."
On Friday, the Dow jumped 263.17 points, or 1.6%, to 16380.41. The S&P 500 gained 24.00 points, or 1.3%,
to 1886.76, and the Nasdaq Composite Index rose 41.05 points, or 1%, to 4258.44. The 10-year U.S.
Treasury note fell 13/32 to yield 2.198%.
Markets have been whipsawed in recent sessions by changing views on global economic growth and
central-bank policy. Early in the week, investors fled risky assets and piled into the relative safety of U.S.
government bonds -- which sent the Dow tumbling as many as 460 points Wednesday, as both trading
volumes and volatility rose to their highest levels in three years. That move was magnified by short-term
investors making sharp adjustments in their asset allocations, traders said. Then, stocks rebounded to close
the week with a sharp gain.
Still, major indexes recorded their fourth consecutive week of declines. The Dow and S&P 500 were both 1%
lower for the week, while the Nasdaq lost 0.4%.
Major benchmarks still haven't closed in a correction, which is typically defined as a decline of 10% or more.
The Dow is 5.2% below its all-time high reached Sept. 19, and the S&P 500 is 6.2% below its Sept. 18 high.
The Nasdaq is 7.4% below its 2014 high hit on Sept. 2.
Treasury prices closed higher for the week. Friday's decline offset much of their striking midweek rally, which
briefly pushed the 10-year yield below 2% amid the heaviest bond trading in more than a decade.
A rally in Europe set a positive tone early Friday, after an ECB official said the bank would start more stimulus
efforts within days and a Bank of England official said rates could stay "lower for longer" than he had
previously thought.
The Stoxx Europe 600 rallied 2.8%. Some of the strongest gains came in Italy and Spain, where stocks
jumped 3.4% and 3%, respectively.
In the U.S., better-than-expected earnings and economic reports also helped give a boost to stocks. The
Commerce Department reported more housing starts in September than expected. And a preliminary reading
on consumer sentiment came in at 86.4, above economists' forecasts of 84.0.
Investors such as Jerry Braakman, chief investment officer at First American Trust, said they were buying
stocks during this week's declines.

"Any more selloff is just going to be an opportunity to purchase stocks, at this point," said Mr. Braakman,

Other oil and gas drillers have also posted sharp swings in recent sessions. QEP Resources, for example,
has seen rollercoaster trading. It dropped more than 20% from where it started the week to its
Wednesday-afternoon low, before rebounding 6.8% Thursday. On Friday, its shares slipped 15 cents, or
0.6%, to 23.18.
Video-streaming company Netflix has been another big mover. On Friday, its shares dropped 4.61, or 1.3%,
to 357.09. That extended a 19% drop from the previous session, on news that it picked up fewer new
subscribers than expected in the third quarter.
Volatility has resurfaced in the U.S. stock market this month, with the CBOE's Volatility Index soaring 55%
since the start of October.
The Dow has staged two other single-session rallies of more than 1% this month, only to erase all of those
gains in the following sessions.

down 13%. It remains above its long-term average of 20, however, after spending most of this year well
below that level.
In commodity markets, crude-oil futures rose 0.1% to $82.75 a barrel. Gold futures fell 0.2% to $1,238.30 an
ounce.

Thursday's Markets
Stocks Bounce Back From Early Tumble

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After days of wild trading, U.S. stocks stabilized as share prices recovered from sharp early losses.
Thursday's trading marked a departure from recent sessions where traders had bailed out of stocks and
scrambled for haven assets such as U.S. government bonds amid worries about slowing global growth and a
sharp drop in oil prices.
Technology and small-company shares, hit hard in recent weeks, ended the session in positive territory as
bargain-hunters bought beaten-down stocks and traders reversed bets that those stocks would fall.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, however, narrowly missed breaking its losing streak, falling for the sixth
session in a row. During this slide, the Dow has lost 876.98 points, or 5.2%.
On Thursday, the Dow fell 24.50 points, or 0.15%, to 16117.24. The blue-chip index was down as much as
206.52 points earlier in the session and then briefly turned positive in early-afternoon trading.
The S&P 500 added 0.27 point, or less than 0.1%, to 1862.76, and the Nasdaq Composite Index inched up
2.07 points, or 0.05%, to 4217.39.
The Russell 2000 index, which had recorded big declines starting in mid-September, gained 13.67 points, or
1.3%, to 1086.11. While Thursday marked the index's third day in a row of gains, the Russell still finished
down 10.1% from its record close in March.
"We're very oversold and due for a bounce," said Jeffrey Yu, head of single-stock derivatives trading at UBS
AG.
Traders pointed to comments from James Bullard, head of the Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis, as helping
stocks rebound from the day's lows. Mr. Bullard said the Fed should consider delaying the end of its
bond-buying program given evidence of falling inflation expectations.
Also helping stocks was a move higher in crude-oil futures, which rose 1.1% to $82.70 a barrel. Energy
stocks rose 1.7%, the biggest sector gain on the S&P 500. Both crude-oil prices and energy stocks have slid
this month.
In the bond market, the yield on the 10-year Treasury note rose to 2.153%, from 2.091% on Wednesday,
snapping a seven-session losing streak as haven buying diminished. Yields fall as prices rise.
Gold futures fell 0.3% to $1,240.50 an ounce.
Signs of stability in U.S. trading followed a wild ride for investors on Wednesday, when the Dow fell as much
as 460 points before partially recovering and the 10-year Treasury yield staged its biggest swing in five years.
Trading on Thursday still was choppy, however, with the Dow crossing the break-even point 24 times.
Volumes were heavy again, with 9.8 billion shares changing hands, a day after the biggest volume session of
the year.
U.S. trading got off to a rocky start after more bad economic news out of Europe, where data showed
eurozone inflation at a five-year low. In Europe, the Stoxx Europe 600 fell 0.4%. The euro weakened to
$1.2809, from $1.2837 late Wednesday.
Early Friday, Japan's Nikkei was flat, Hong Kong's Hang Seng index was up 0.4% and Australia's S&P/ASX
200 was up 0.5%.
