Global Finance: A Year On, Shanghai Zone Disappoints
Global Finance: A Year On, Shanghai Zone Disappoints
By Shen Hong
927 words
29 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SHANGHAI -- One year after the launch of the Shanghai free-trade zone, promoted as a landmark effort to
remake China's economy, the project has generated few significant reforms.
While there have been changes, including cutting some red tape for businesses and opening up certain
industries to investment, executives say the overhauls don't amount to much. The removal of the project's
leader, Dai Haibo, on Sept. 15 marked the latest setback for the free-trade zone.
"Progress on the Shanghai FTZ has been quite modest, even by the low standards of China's gradual and
incremental approach to market-oriented and financial-sector reforms," said Eswar Prasad, a China expert at
Cornell University.
Promoted by Premier Li Keqiang as a symbol of China's commitment to reform, the Shanghai free-trade
zone, launched on Sept. 29, 2013, initially fueled hopes for a repeat of the Shenzhen special economic zone
in the 1980s, which transformed the fishing village into the heart of China's export machine.
The Shanghai zone encompasses nearly 11 square miles of docks, hangars and warehouses in Shanghai's
Pudong district. It was promoted as an incubator for financial revamps such as interest-rate liberalization and
easier cross-border capital flows that eventually would spill over the entire country.
Instead, the most visible change inside the zone is cheap, directly imported seafood. Waiting in lines that are
often over 100 yards long, customers snap up frozen Mozambique lobsters, Chilean king crabs and
Vietnamese tiger prawns, which are usually sold out within an hour after opening.
There have been some overhauls in the free-trade zone, including a reduction in red tape for business
registration, minimizing bureaucracy at customs, and opening up investments for foreign capital in areas such
as health care and engineering.
But business executives aren't impressed. "I still don't find the FTZ as a physical entity very exciting because
one year on, I would expect more to have happened," said Fredrik Hahnel, general manager of merchant
banking at the Shanghai branch of Nordic lender Skandinaviska Enskilda Banken AB.
Mr. Hahnel said he had hoped for breakthroughs such as permission for companies to issue
yuan-denominated bonds in the free-trade zone and full access for wholly owned foreign investment banks or
brokerage firms to China's domestic capital markets.
Mr. Hahnel said SEB isn't considering setting up a branch in the free-zone now. "We don't see anything we
can do in the FTZ that we can't do outside now. . .. But if we see fundamental changes, such as total access
to offshore funding or the capital markets, we'll look into it," he said.
Other modest overhauls include a measure to allow companies registered in the zone to borrow the Chinese
currency from offshore and another allowing companies to transfer yuan-denominated funds between their
entities registered in the free-trade zone and those offshore or in the mainland. In March, the central bank
also removed the upper limit on foreign-currency deposit rates offered by lenders in the zone.
Those moves are in contrast to the ambitious vision that regulators initially painted for the reform testing
ground. In addition to proposals for giving banks broad freedom to set interest rates and trade in foreign
exchange, there were plans to build marketplaces in the zone for trading of financial derivatives and
foreign-company shares. The changes have fallen far short of those expectations.
Two weeks ago, as the one-year anniversary of the free-trade zone approached, the government said the
leader of the zone, Mr. Dai, had been removed, without giving a reason. State-run media have reported that
he is under investigation for alleged corruption. On Friday, Shanghai's vice mayor, Ai Baojun, said at a news
conference that Mr. Dai's removal was a "normal personnel shift."

--- Page 80 ---
Page 80 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The latest effort to put some shine on the free-trade zone is an international trading platform at the Shanghai
Gold Exchange, which will allow investors to price and trade the precious metal using the Chinese currency.
Hours after Mr. Dai was stripped of his post, the government said the launch date would be moved up to
Sept. 18 instead of on Monday's anniversary.
The debut of the new gold-trading platform coincided with a surprise visit to the zone by Premier Li, whose
failure to appear at the zone's launch spurred speculation that Beijing was scaling back its reform ambitions.
There have been other encouraging signs lately. Microsoft Corp. said on Thursday that its new game
console, the Xbox One, will go on sale through the Shanghai free-trade zone on Sept. 29. It will become the
first game console to be legally sold in mainland China after the country lifted a ban that lasted more than a
decade.
But the good news is overshadowed by what still isn't allowed in the free-trade zone. Beijing has kept a long
"negative list" that details industries that are off limits to foreign investors. The government did cut the number
to 139 from 190 in July, lifting barriers to foreign investment in banks and other financial companies, land,
Internet cafes and rail-freight transport. But business executives say the list is still too long.
"I think they must be concerned that the free-trade zone is some kind of Trojan horse that could breach the
capital controls and become a source of instability. Caution prevails again," said Tim Condon, an economist
at ING.

Platinum fell to a nearly five-year low and gold ended at its weakest price this year, hit by a rising dollar and

China and Japan would curb demand for cars hurt platinum prices. Platinum and its sister metal, palladium,

The ICE U.S. Dollar Index extended its longest winning streak in at least 28 years on Friday, as it rose for an
11th straight week against a basket of major currencies. Investors have piled into the greenback in recent
weeks, betting the Federal Reserve is closer to raising interest rates, a move that would make the dollar
more attractive to yield-seeking investors. That is bad news for gold, which investors often buy as a hedge
against a weaker U.S. currency and which struggles to compete with other assets when interest rates rise.
While investors often use gold as a hedge against economic or political turmoil, "platinum is more of a pure
economic play," said James Cordier, a principal at Liberty Trading. "At the moment, it appears that the U.S.
economy is doing well, while the rest of the world is in the doldrums."
The gap between gold and platinum prices narrowed to $86.60, the lowest this year. The contracting spread
could lead investors to use a strategy where they buy platinum and sell gold, betting platinum will outperform
its counterpart, traders said.
Palladium prices also were plagued by worries about stockpiles in Russia. Thursday, they fell 2% after
investors reversed their view of how a potential sale of the metal by the Russian government to OAO Norilsk
Nickel, the world's largest palladium producer, would affect prices. Speculation that government stores were
nearly exhausted had helped support prices in recent years. But the size of the proposed deal with Norilsk, at
nearly $2 billion, revived talk that the government's hoard is larger than was thought.

Global Anxiety Roils Markets
Global Anxiety Roils Markets
By E.S. Browning
1,092 words
26 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Money managers began issuing the warnings late last year: Financial markets would be more volatile in 2014
than in 2013. They were right.
After posting sharp but limited declines in January, April and July, the Dow Jones Industrial Average on
Thursday fell 264.26 points, or 1.5%, to 16945.80, its largest one-day point and percentage decline since July
31. It was the third day of declines this week, but because the Dow hit a record last Friday, it still is only 1.9%
off that record. Other major indexes also declined, and stocks fell in early trading Friday in Asia.
Traders and money managers blamed the same things that have roiled markets before: high stock prices, the
uncertain global economy and international conflicts.
Of particular concern this time, some investors said, were reports from Moscow that a pro-Kremlin legislator
wants to allow the seizure of foreign assets. Actual asset seizures could push Europe closer to recession,
especially those countries with close economic relations with Russia. Just the threat of such seizures could
further escalate tensions over Ukraine.
"What is behind it is mainly the news out of Russia about the Russian lawmakers drafting legislation allowing
the government there to seize foreign assets in response to the sanctions against Russia," said Leo
Grohowski, chief investment officer BNY Mellon Wealth Management, which oversees about $190 billion in
New York.
U.S. Treasury-bond prices rose as investors shifted money toward their relative safety. The 10-year Treasury
note rose 16/32 of a point in price. Its yield, which falls when the price rises, fell 0.056 percentage point to
2.511%, the biggest one-day decline since July 17. Gold, another refuge, also moved higher, although it
remains 35% off its nominal record, posted in 2011. Many foreign stock markets declined, including those of
Britain, Germany and France.
"We haven't seen a day like this in a while," said Rex Macey, chief allocation officer at Wilmington Trust
Investment Advisors, which oversees $20 billion. Like many money managers, he compared this pullback to
the ones that came earlier this year. He, like many others, said he once again would be surprised if it reached
10% before it ended.
"The economy is just doing too well, and profits are doing too well, for the kind of 10% correction you might
think about," he said.
This time, however, there is an additional problem: Investors are concerned about expectations that the
Federal Reserve will start raising short-term interest rates next year for the first time since 2006. On top of
that, the Fed plans to end its huge bond-buying stimulus program in November.
Those two shifts mark a gradual stiffening of the Fed's long-favorable financial policy. While the interest-rate
backdrop remains unusually supportive for the economy and markets, it is becoming less so.
"Higher interest rates are something that the market hasn't had to deal with for many, many years, and the
impact could be more significant than what we are seeing currently," said Mr. Grohowski of BNY Mellon.
Junk bonds, the debt of low-rated companies, also took a hit. Some investors worried that, if interest rates
rise, junk-bond default rates could rise. Forty-two of the 50 most actively traded junk bonds fell in value
Thursday, with debt issued by the riskier among them recording the largest losses, according to
MarketAxess.
In addition to reports from Russia, investors said traders might have been spooked by news that orders for
big-ticket goods fell in July and unemployment claims rose in the past week. Neither number was much of a
surprise, however, and money managers said they hardly explained the large drop.

--- Page 83 ---
Page 83 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Trading in New York Stock Exchange-listed stocks reached 3.26 billion shares, just above this year's
average, but it was probably held down by the Rosh Hashana holiday.
Investors remain concerned about the continuing risk of recession in Europe and about the possibility that
Chinese growth may be slower in coming months than it was in past years. They worry about Russia and
about the new U.S. involvement in the Middle East.
They also worry about the strengthening dollar, which makes U.S. exports more expensive and foreign
income to U.S. multinationals less valuable. The dollar late Thursday in New York was at its highest level
against the euro since November 2012.
Investors also are worried about stock prices themselves.
After this year's earlier episodes of volatility, stocks rebounded to new highs, which means stock prices are
high now compared with underlying corporate earnings.
The S&P 500, which declined 32.31 points, or 1.6%, on Thursday, still trades at 19 times its companies' net
profits for the past 12 months, according to calculations by Birinyi Associates. That is higher than the
long-term average of 15.5. Investors often use a different measure called operating earnings, which excludes
extraordinary earnings items, but even with that measure, the price/earnings ratio is 18, Birinyi said.
Although few money managers find it alarming, they say it will be hard for the P/E ratio to move much higher.
If that is right, it means future stock gains could simply keep pace with corporate earnings, meaning around
8% to 10% a year counting dividends.
One major reason that few money managers expect large stock declines is that they expect the U.S.
economy to keep growing solidly, if unspectacularly. Forecasts call for 2.5% to 3% growth in 2014, including
the rocky first quarter.
"We believe the domestic economy continues to be OK -- not great, but OK," said Lew Piantedosi, who
oversees about $11 billion at asset-manager Eaton Vance. "It's two steps up, one-and-a-half steps back. I
don't think there's going to be any change to that given the environment we find ourselves in."
Moreover, with inflation still running below the Fed's 2% annual target, the U.S. central bank is under no
pressure to raise interest rates quickly, meaning that few investors fear a recession in the U.S.
So far this year, the biggest pullback in the Dow industrials was 7.3% in January and early February. Some
money managers think a larger dip could be in store. The most common fear, however, is a stock pullback of
5% to 15%, not a prolonged market decline or a new financial crisis.

Once Again, Oracle Must Reinvent Itself
Once Again, Oracle Must Reinvent Itself
By Shira Ovide
1,128 words
20 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Through 37 years, Oracle Corp. Chief Executive Larry Ellison was a master of corporate reinvention as his
company navigated constant technological change. But today the global database powerhouse he built faces
challenges as severe as any in its history, and Mr. Ellison's departure as CEO only intensifies the central
issue surrounding the company's future: Can Oracle endure recent tectonic shifts that are reshaping its
market?
In dividing the chief's executive's role between lieutenants Mark Hurd and Safra Catz, Mr. Ellison is handing
his new co-CEOs a $185 billion software empire that is under assault from technology and market forces that
Mr. Ellison couldn't have anticipated when he founded the company in the 1970s.
Oracle can no longer count on its near-monopoly in database systems, as the emerging technologies of big
data and cloud computing -- often available in open source versions that cost far less to use -- along with a
customer base eager for alternatives, fragment the market. At the same time, younger competitors such as
Salesforce.com Inc. and Workday Inc. are picking off Oracle customers by offering specialized software
applications sold by subscription rather than in a large lump sum plus a service contract.
Tech industry executives and investors say Oracle needs to move faster to adapt its technology and sales
approach, or risk losing its grip on the market.
"There is a new computing paradigm that Oracle is not participating in, and that's big data and software as a
service," said Peter Goldmacher, a former Wall Street technology analyst who is now an industry strategy
consultant.
These areas are growing more than 20% a year, according to research firm IDC, and Oracle is a niche player
in both. The hardball sales tactics that helped make Oracle the dominant database vendor may be a liability
as more, and more capable, alternatives emerge.
The challenges are showing up in Oracle's financial results. The company's annual revenue has increased
4.2% or less in each of the past three fiscal years. Cloud software revenue rose 31% in the three months
ended in August, but it remains less than 6% of total sales. Oracle's operating margin fell in the most recent
quarter, the first year-over-year decline since it acquired Sun Microsystems in early 2010.
In an interview Friday, Mr. Hurd said Oracle is posting sales growth in important emerging areas of
technology, while other major corporate-technology vendors are growing slowly or not at all. Mr. Hurd also
said the job of Oracle's sales force is to "align with customers' strategies."
The big question is whether the sales lull is temporary or a sign that competition is catching up. The
company's database has been the gold standard in the field for decades. But newer technologies, honed at
places like Google Inc. and Facebook Inc., are better suited and more cost-effective than Oracle's for
handling big data, a phrase that describes the flood of digital information generated by web traffic, sensors,
networked communications, and Internet-connected machines.
For instance, Aeris Communications Inc. uses a free database technology known as Apache Cassandra to
gather data and analyze it in ways for which Oracle's products are ill-suited. Aeris crunches data from electric
meters, for example, and alerts utility companies of usage patterns that may signal a problem with the power
grid.
Oracle software couldn't handle the flood of data at a reasonable cost, said Drew Johnson, Aeris's vice
president of engineering.
"Fifteen years ago, there was no question: If you want to store data, you go to Oracle," Mr. Johnson said. "I
ask my [software] architects, 'Build something to handle a billion things.' When you look at that kind of scale,
Oracle is no longer the best."

--- Page 89 ---
Page 89 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
As companies allocate money for alternatives to Oracle, they push the company out of these growth areas.
Spending on big-data analytics will increase from about $10 billion in 2012 to more than $32 billion in 2017,
according to IDC. The 27% compound annual growth rate is far faster than the expected overall spending on
corporate software.
An even bigger challenge is software applications such as the PeopleSoft human-resources technology
Oracle acquired a decade ago. More companies are opting to pay for such software as a subscription
accessed from remote computer, rather than buy the software to install on their own computer servers.
Oracle had a 2.8% share of the market for cloud software in 2013, according to IDC, a smaller share than
Salesforce.com and even aging tech companies such as Microsoft Corp. and Automatic Data Processing.
Oracle is striving to adapt. It has increased spending on research and development, including a 7% bump in
the quarter ended Aug. 31, and it has received good reviews for a new type of database that manages
so-called transactional information, such as the progress of parts as they move through a factory. Mr. Ellison
is expected to introduce in about a week a much-anticipated offering of Oracle's database as a cloud service.
Despite efforts to revamp its technology, Oracle is meeting resistance from customers who are fed up with
the company's aggressive sales strategy. Samuel Chesterman, chief information officer of the marketing firm
IPG Mediabrands, says he called his Oracle sales representative a few years ago -- when he was working
with another company -- to drop three Oracle is meeting resistance from customers who are fed up with the
company's aggressive sales strategy. Samuel Chesterman, chief information officer of the marketing firm IPG
Mediabrands, says he called his Oracle sales representative a few years ago -- when he was working with
another company -- to drop three HR products the company wasn't using. Mr. Chesterman was shocked to
learn the move would increase his Oracle bill by $50,000 a year, minus licensing and support for those three
products.
"I've been doing this for 20 years and no company but Oracle has done this to me," said Mr. Chesterman.
"Oracle definitely plays a heavy hand with their pricing."
An Oracle spokesperson said the customer's experience wasn't representative of the company's sales
practices.
In any case, the vast majority of Oracle's 400,000 customers find their databases too crucial to ditch anytime
soon, technology buyers say. Oracle executives, and even many of the company's critics, say its software is
so essential, and its tens of thousands of sales people so adroit, that the company can negotiate changing
technology winds better than other Silicon Valley pioneers.
"All the legacy guys are going to die," said Mr. Goldmacher. "These guys are going to die last."

Dow Hits a Record, Buoyed by the Fed

Dow Hits a Record, Buoyed by the Fed

The five-year bull market in U.S. stocks got a new lease on life, as investors embrace the steady-as-she-goes
message the Federal Reserve is delivering on the economy and interest rates.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose to a record and the dollar jumped to a six-year high against the yen,
after the Fed took tentative steps toward unwinding its historic easy-money policies while reassuring
investors that rates will remain low even as the economy expands.
The gains were accompanied by a decline in U.S. government-bond prices that sent Treasury yields higher
and a selloff in many emerging-market stocks and currencies. Assets in poorer nations often are hit first when
interest rates rise.
Wednesday's action underscores the investor belief that the Fed will raise interest rates next year, while the
European Central Bank and Bank of Japan will continue pushing rates down in a bid to spur struggling
economies.
"In the big picture, the Fed is decreasing its balance sheet and looking to tighten policy, while the ECB and
Bank of Japan are on the opposite path," said Kiran Ganesh, a strategist at UBS Wealth Management.
The Dow industrials gained 24.88 points, or 0.1%, to 17156.85. That marked the blue chips' 16th record of
the year and leaves the index up 3.5% in 2014.
Investors said the Fed meeting reinforced demand for riskier investments such as stocks, and bolstered the
case for a stronger U.S. dollar. U.S. government bonds are seen as expensive, but also unlikely to post sharp
declines, with the Fed taking a measured approach toward pulling back on its easy-money policies.
Ahead of Wednesday's statement by the Fed, investors had been looking for hints as to how rapidly the
central bank would proceed toward reversing its easing efforts. Fed policy has been credited with fueling the
five-year-long bull market in stocks and keeping bond yields low.
In its policy statement, the Fed said it expected to keep short-term interest rates near zero for a
"considerable time."
However, the Fed also confirmed that it would end its bond-buying program next month and offered new
details on the mechanism it plans to use to raise interest rates when that time comes.
"They're still working to keep rates low," said Robert Pavlik, chief market strategist at Banyan Partners, which
oversees about $4.5 billion in assets. "They're not moving on interest rates, so the overall equity market
should be pleased with that.
"You wouldn't see a dramatic uptick in the bond-market yield because they're giving you a pretty dovish
stance," he said.
Mr. Pavlik has reduced the cash holdings in his portfolio in recent weeks. He has tilted his holdings toward
stocks that would particularly benefit from accelerating economic growth because of what he sees as a
strengthening U.S. economy.
The S&P 500 index fell just short of a record, rising 2.59 points, or 0.1%, to 2001.57, just 0.3% off its high of
2007.71 hit Sept. 5.
The Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 9.43 points, or 0.2%, to 4562.19.

Peso Hits New Low On Fall in Reserves
By Ken Parks
540 words
17 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BUENOS AIRES -- The Argentine peso hit record lows against the U.S. dollar in black-market trading after
the South American country's central bank reported the biggest one-day fall in its international reserves
since the government defaulted on some of its debt July 30.
The peso weakened to 14.72 per dollar on the black market, from 14.47 per dollar on Monday, according to
the newspaper El Cronista, which publishes rates collected from underground currency dealers.
The peso's rout came after the central bank said its stockpile of foreign currencies and gold fell by $184
million on Monday to a 4 1/2-month low of $28.2 billion.
The drop in reserves raised fears that President Cristina Kirchner's government might run out of the dollars it
needs to pay creditors and the country's import bill. That would mean more pain for an economy that already
is expected to contract 2.1% amid inflation of 37.4% this year, according to the latest survey of analysts by
FocusEconomics.
The Argentine currency closed unchanged at 8.4030 per dollar on the tightly regulated official market
Tuesday.
"Volume was greater than normal. Today, it was $216 million when yesterday it was $160 million," said
Francisco Diaz, a currency trader at ABC Mercado de Cambio. "There might have been a big export deal that
allowed the central bank to buy [dollars]."
The peso has weakened about 2.5% on the official currency market since the default, compared with its 16%
slide on the black market.
The widening gap between exchange rates has fueled speculation that the government might devalue the
peso for a second time this year following a 20% devaluation in January. The local Rofex futures market
quoted the peso at 9.0970 to the dollar in December.
With the peso fast approaching 15 per dollar, locals may soon be talking about the Dolar Demichelis, named
after Argentine soccer player Martin Demichelis's jersey number. Argentines already joke that their currency
has gone this year from the Dolar Messi to the Dolar Mascherano -- a reference to the number 10 worn by
soccer superstar Lionel Messi to the number 14 worn by the midfielder Javier Mascherano.
Mrs. Kirchner's cabinet chief, Jorge Capitanich, attributed Monday's drop in reserves to fuel and debt
payments.
Reserves have fallen from a post devaluation peak of $29.7 billion on July 24 as the seasonal dollar surge
from the soy harvest -- Argentina's largest source of hard currency -- comes to an end. With few prospects of
significant dollar inflows until the next harvest, the country faces lean months ahead.
International credit markets remain firmly shut to Argentina owing to the default, which stems from a legal
fight between Buenos Aires and creditors suing to collect on bonds the country stopped paying in 2001.
Unable to borrow and with no immediate solution to default on the horizon, the Kirchner administration has
moved to limit dollar sales to the public.
Earlier in September, the central bank ordered banks to file a report anytime a company tries to buy more
than $150,000 in dollars from the previous threshold of $300,000.

Bears Turn Docile as Market Keeps on Roaring --- With Fed at Helm, Investors Traveling A Steady Course In Uneasy World
Bears Turn Docile as Market Keeps on Roaring --- With Fed at Helm, Investors Traveling A Steady
Course In Uneasy World
By E.S. Browning
1,043 words
15 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The world is a scary place for investors.
Market fundamentals, from stock prices to interest rates and profit margins, are stretched. Europe is flirting
with recession, the U.S. is expanding its Middle East bombing, and Ukraine and Gaza are enduring uneasy
truces.
And financial markets act as if everything is just fine. Investors widely believe the Federal Reserve and other
central banks will do what it takes to keep economies and financial markets healthy.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average and the S&P 500 are 1% from their records. Among the others that are
near records, India and Canada are 1% away, Argentina is at a record and London is 2% away. Shanghai is
at a 52-week high. A wide variety of U.S. and foreign bonds have higher prices, and lower yields, than in
January. Among the few investments that have been suffering is gold, a refuge for the fearful.
Of course, stocks and bonds could sour this fall. September historically has often been a bad month for
stocks. Money managers have been predicting a pullback in stock and bond prices for a year. So far, there is
little sign of it.
The most common explanation is that, five years into the economic recovery, money managers have come to
rely on the Federal Reserve and other central banks to keep financial conditions favorable.
"Policy makers globally are still providing substantial support to financial markets," noted Krishna Memani,
chief investment officer at OppenheimerFunds Inc., which oversees $251 billion in New York.
While the Fed is widely expected to raise short-term interest rates next year, Mr. Memani says it could move
more slowly than many expect. Economists forecast that the Fed will start in mid-2015, but Mr. Memani says
it could delay if it sees economic weakness, and will act very gradually once it begins. The Fed's bond-buying
program is almost over, with no apparent damage to markets.
As for the risk of more Washington gridlock after the November elections, Mr. Memani says markets expect
very little from politicians.
"The help markets are expecting is from the Federal Reserve, and as long as the Federal Reserve is on their
side they believe they will be OK," he said.
This backdrop doesn't delight everyone.
When the Fed plays such a strong role, "you aren't allowing the free markets to really assert their will," said
Jason Trennert, founder of Strategas Research Partners in New York. That makes many professional
investors uneasy because it distorts markets, creating the stretched stock and bond valuations investors are
dealing with today.
Still, Mr. Trennert, too, said he expects stocks to move higher, "simply because there is a paucity of other
alternatives." British Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher defended free markets by saying, "There is no
alternative." Mr. Trennert says the same "Tina" rule applies to the stock market today.
The signs of a stressed market are widespread. After-tax S&P 500 corporate profit margins hit 9.8% in the
second quarter, the highest in at least 20 years, according to data tracked by Mr. Trennert. That is well above
the 9.3% peak in 2007, and the 7.8% peak in 2000. The higher margins go, the bigger the risk of a decline.

--- Page 94 ---
Page 94 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Low interest rates make bond returns unappetizing, pulling money toward stocks. And it is cheap to invest
with borrowed money. This helps explain why the S&P 500 trades at 19 times its companies' net profits for
the past 12 months, well above the long-term average of 15.5, according to Birinyi Associates.
The Fed pushed short-term interest rates close to zero back in 2008, to force investors and companies to
put money to work and help pull the economy out of recession.
It worked, but the Fed considers the job unfinished.
The Fed's favorite inflation indicator, based on consumer spending, is running at only about 1.5% a year,
well below the Fed's 2% target. Wage gains are running around 2.5% for nonmanagement employees, well
below the Fed's target of 3% to 4%. This low inflation leaves Fed officials free to help the economy by
raising rates very gradually.
Mark Freeman, chief investment officer of Westwood Holdings Group, which oversees $20.1 billion in Dallas,
has a fund that can shift money among different kinds of investments. Currently, it is 18% in cash, not
because Mr. Freeman has cut his stock holding but because he has pulled back from high-priced,
low-yielding bonds. His stock allocation, at about 50%, is the highest it has ever been in that fund, he says.
"If you listen to what [Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen] is saying, I think she is going to be highly disinclined to
raise rates until wage growth gets closer to 3% or 4%" from the current 2.5%, he said. "If those variables don't
change, the Fed will remain very, very accommodative." Fed policy makers meet next on Tuesday and
Wednesday.
With inflation and interest rates low and economic growth moderate, Mr. Freeman foresees steady
corporate-profit growth. High price-earnings ratios mean stock prices may not surge from here, Mr. Freeman
says, but he adds that stocks can rise around 8% or 9% a year, in line with earnings gains.
As for war and politics, markets are good "at differentiating between political events and economic events. A
lot of the items currently on the list of worries are in the political category rather than the economic category,"
Mr. Freeman said. Oil prices, for example, have been falling despite the Middle East fighting and the worries
that Russia could disrupt natural-gas deliveries to Europe. That suggests markets don't expect global
tensions to damage energy supplies too much.
What could change this rosy picture?
"The single biggest risk to a continuation of the bull market in my view is inflation," said Mr. Trennert of
Strategas. "And there are few signs of it being imminent."

September 15, 2014)

Resurgent Dollar Fuels Rally --- Longest Winning Streak in 17 Years Helps Drive Demand for U.S.

By Ira Iosebashvili, Dan Strumpf and Min Zeng

English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A resurgent dollar is bolstering global demand for U.S. stocks and bonds as investors flock to a recovering
American economy.
The dollar has logged its longest winning streak in more than 17 years, rising against a broad basket of
currencies for nine straight weeks, according to the ICE U.S. Dollar Index.
The gains reflect investor expectations that the Federal Reserve next year will raise its fed-funds rate for the
first time since 2006, while central bankers in Europe and Japan will seek to spur stagnant economies by
holding down interest rates and adopting other easy-money policies.
The prospect of higher U.S. rates is drawing investors from around the globe in search of stronger returns
amid near-zero interest rates. At the same time, the dollar rally is pushing down prices of globally traded
commodities such as oil and gold.
"The U.S. economy just looks so much better than anywhere else, and this is driving capital flows toward the
dollar," said James Kwok, head of currency management at Amundi, a Paris-based investment fund
managing around $1.1 trillion.
In late Friday New York trading, a dollar purchased 107.35 yen, its highest level against the Japanese
currency since October 2008. The WSJ Dollar Index has risen 3.3% this year and is up 17% since hitting a
2011 low.
Investors have been buying the dollar ahead of the Federal Open Market Committee meeting set to conclude
Wednesday, when the U.S. central bank could next update its interest-rate outlook.
Mr. Kwok said he is buying the dollar against the Australian dollar, the Canadian dollar and the Swiss franc.
A rising dollar often creates a virtuous circle for the U.S. economy. Dollar gains signal a perception of
economic health. Also, by making imports including oil cheaper, they can boost U.S. growth by increasing
consumer spending power.
Consumer spending, which accounts for the bulk of U.S. economic activity, has grown anemically since the
financial crisis amid high unemployment and soft wage growth.
Bill Bell, who helps oversee $9.7 billion as a portfolio manager at Atlanta Capital Management, said he has
been buying more shares of domestically focused U.S. firms such as retailers. A stronger dollar "creates a
tailwind for the U.S. consumer because their cost of living goes down," he said.
Many analysts and traders say the dollar's recent gains are reminiscent of the late 1990s -- a period in which
the dollar rallied sharply during a record run for U.S. stocks. Most shrug off comparisons to the last significant
dollar rally, a 2008 increase that accompanied the global flight to safe assets such as U.S. Treasury bonds
during the financial crisis.
From the start of this year through Sept. 10, investors poured $111.6 billion into U.S. stocks and bonds,
according to data tracker EPFR Global. That compares with $89.4 billion in the year-earlier period, which
included a substantial outflow from U.S. bonds amid a mid-2013 rise in interest rates.
The dollar rally comes as stock and bond prices have risen in the U.S., confounding many analysts who
expected bond prices to fall as the Fed reduced its stimulus though a process known as tapering. The Dow
Jones Industrial Average fell 61.49 to 16987.51 on Friday, putting its year-to-date gain at 2.5%.

--- Page 96 ---
Page 96 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The 10-year U.S. Treasury yield rose Friday to 2.61%, the eighth gain in 10 days, a sign that interest-rate
expectations are rising. A sustained rise in rates would hit bond prices.
Gold fell $7.50 Friday to $1229.90, while New York crude-oil futures fell 56 cents a barrel to $92.27. Both
become more expensive for foreign buyers in home-currency terms when the dollar strengthens.
In recent weeks, the Japanese yen has hit a six-year low and the euro tumbled to its lowest level in 14
months. The dollar notched multimonth highs against South Africa's rand and the Turkish lira.
The dollar's surge "is long overdue," said Ugo Lancioni, a managing director at Neuberger Berman
overseeing some $16 billion in assets.
Mr. Lancioni has been buying the U.S. dollar against the Australian and New Zealand dollars -- two
currencies that benefited from investors seeking to capture the spreads between the relatively high interest
rates in those two countries and the rock-bottom borrowing costs on U.S. dollar assets, in a strategy known
as a carry trade.
Mr. Lancioni said he expects that difference to shrink as the Fed moves closer to tightening monetary policy,
making the trade less profitable,
A stronger dollar isn't a panacea. It makes U.S. goods more expensive abroad, likely slowing the growth of
U.S. exports such as automobiles, car parts and capital goods, and putting pressure on the trade deficit. A
smaller trade deficit generally helps the economy over the long term because it means a growing share of
money in the U.S. is being spent on goods and services domestically rather than abroad.
Atlanta Capital Management's Mr. Bell said companies in his portfolios now derive about 15% to 20% of their
sales from non-U.S. markets, compared with 20% to 25% historically.
Other investors and analysts caution that a rallying U.S. dollar could pose problems for emerging-market
countries.
"Rising rates, and more expensive access to capital, create challenges for leveraged issuers and riskier
economies," said Jonathan Lewis, chief investment officer at Samson Capital Advisors LLC, which has $7.4
billion assets under management.
Some investors say the costs of dollar strength are worth bearing because slower growth means less reason
to fear an uptick in inflation, which takes a heavy toll on fixed-income investments in particular.
Not all investors believe the currency market is at the cusp of a new, dollar-driven era.
Eric Lascelles, chief economist at RBC Global Asset Management Inc., which has $305 billion in assets
under management, said he expects the U.S. dollar to continue strengthening, though not as rapidly as in
1994. "The Fed is unlikely to act as forcefully in its tightening, given a fervent desire not to snuff out this
tentative recovery,"" he said.
Lower-than-expected rates will detract from the dollar's attractiveness to yield-seeking investors, said Michael
Collins, senior portfolio manager at Prudential Fixed Income, which has $533 billion assets under
management.
"There is too much tightening priced in on the U.S. side, too much easing priced in on the euro side," said Mr.
Collins. "It's a big global world, and it will be hard for the U.S. to steam ahead while the rest of the world is
sluggish."

U.S. stocks fell Friday, snapping a streak of five consecutive weekly gains, amid growing anxiety that Federal

On the week, the Dow fell 0.9% and the S&P 500 dropped 1.1%. In each case, the weekly loss was the first
for the index since the week ended Aug. 1. For the year, the Dow industrials have gained 2.5% and the S&P

On the heels of stocks' run to recent highs, many investors say U.S. companies' shares are already
appropriately valued and due for a pullback. Moreover, some say stocks could see some choppiness if the
Fed raises interest rates sooner than expected, reversing an accommodative stance widely believed to have
added fuel to stocks' rally in recent years.
A two-day meeting of the Fed's policy-setting committee is scheduled to wrap up Wednesday, followed by a
press conference by Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen, offering money managers their next glimpse into the
central bank's plans.
"We sense a lot of investor nervousness and we've been taking a little risk off the table," said Alan Gayle,
director of asset allocation at RidgeWorth Investments, which oversees about $50 billion.
Anxiety about interest rates fueled selling in bonds, pushing the yield on the 10-year U.S. Treasury note up
to 2.612%. Bond yields rise as prices fall.
The 10-year Treasury yield rose by 0.15 percentage point this week, the most on a weekly basis since
September 2013.
Many investors expect the Fed to raise rates next year for the first time since 2006. Higher rates tend to
weigh on bond prices.
"It is a potential game-changer for the bond market if the Fed changes the language to indicate a quicker
tightening time frame,"" said Larry Milstein, head of government and agency trading at R.W. Pressprich & Co.
in New York. "If investors feel the party is over, they could head for the exits."
Stocks seen as particularly vulnerable to rising interest rates underperformed on Friday. The MSCI U.S.
REIT index dropped 3.2%.
Shares of banks, seen as potential beneficiaries of rising rates, outperformed. Goldman Sachs Group led the
Dow, gaining 1.2%, or $2.17, to $183.17, followed by J.P. Morgan Chase, which advanced 27 cents, or 0.5%,
to 60.03.
"Everyone's fearing interest rates going up and reality is starting to set in," said Wayne Lin, a portfolio
manager at QS Investors, which oversees about $20.5 billion. Still, he has maintained a bullish positioning on
U.S. stocks, despite seeing them as "fully valued," because valuations are even less attractive in fixed
income. As a result, stocks are likely to outperform bonds, he said.
On the economic front, August retail sales rose 0.6% from July, the Commerce Department reported Friday,
just shy of the 0.7% median forecast of economists in a Wall Street Journal poll. Separately, import prices fell
0.9% in August from July, in line with expectations.

Gold Falls to 8-Month Low
By Tatyana Shumsky
518 words
13 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold prices ended at the lowest level in eight months, as the dollar continued its advance against other
currencies.
Gold for December delivery, the most actively traded contract, fell $7.50, or 0.6%, to $1,231.50 a troy ounce
on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange on Friday, its lowest settlement since Jan. 9.
Gold prices slumped 2.8% this week as the ICE Dollar Index, which tracks the dollar against a basket of
other currencies, vaulted to a 13-month high. The greenback made advances against both the euro and the
yen, climbing close to a six-year high against Japan's currency on Friday.
"The precious metals complex is just completely falling apart, and a lot of it is attributable to the stronger
dollar and that interest rates will be higher in the coming months," said John Payne, senior market analyst
with Daniels Trading in Chicago.
Gold is traded in dollars and becomes more expensive for investors who use other currencies to fund their
purchases, deterring those buyers from the market. The precious metal is also seen as an alternative to other
haven investments, like Treasury bonds, but could struggle to attract investors if interest rates climb
because it offers no yield.
The Federal Reserve is widely expected to end its bond-buying program in October, paving the way for the
central bank to raise interest rates in 2015. It would be the first move to tighten monetary policy since
2006.
Fading concerns over a potential war between Russia and Ukraine have also stemmed investor appetite for
gold as a haven. Some traders believe the precious metal would hold its value better than other assets
during periods of political upheaval, but their interest in gold fades as such threats dissipate.
In other commodity markets:
COPPER: Copper futures edged higher as signs of improvement in the U.S. and European economies
helped offset pressure from a stronger dollar.
The most actively traded contract, for December delivery, rose 1.40 cents, or 0.5%, to settle at $3.1065 a
pound on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
The U.S. and Europe are large copper consumers, but lag top copper buyer China. Still, investors follow
economic data from both countries for clues about future metal demand.
GRAINS: Wheat prices finished lower, capping a weeklong slide.
Wheat for December delivery, the most-actively traded contract, declined for the fifth consecutive session on
the Chicago Board of Trade, shedding seven cents, or 1.4%, to $5.025 a bushel. Wheat prices have dropped
22% this year.
The U.S. Department of Agriculture on Thursday estimated that global wheat stockpiles for the 2014-15
season would total 196.38 million metric tons, topping analyst estimates of about 193 million tons.
Soybean futures rose, helped in part by continued signs of strong demand for the oilseeds. Worry that
excessive rains in the western Farm Belt could lead to crop damage also lent support. November soybean
futures rose 0.4% to $9.8525 a bushel.

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- Expecting Trouble? Here Are Investments to Ponder
Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- Expecting Trouble? Here Are Investments to
Ponder
By Erin McCarthy
681 words
8 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It may be time for investors to seek out safe harbors.
Global conflicts, an early August stock swoon and expectations that the Federal Reserve will soon end the
economic-stimulus program that has helped prop up the stock market have strengthened the case for adding
safe-haven investments such as shorter-term U.S. government bonds, gold and currencies to portfolios.
"If there's an external shock to the system, whether it's an expansion of hostilities in Ukraine or Iraq, we are to
a point where the market could take it very hard," says Will Hepburn, president of Hepburn Capital
Management in Prescott, Ariz.
Advisers and analysts point to several assets and related mutual funds and exchange-traded funds that could
help reduce exposure to a stock-market pullback. Of course, before deciding which route to take, investors
need to think about what worries them more: losing opportunities or losing money.
-- Bonds: When it comes to safe-haven investments, high-quality, shorter-term bonds, including U.S.
government bonds and corporate debt, top many investors' lists. Mr. Hepburn likes iShares 7-10 Year
Treasury Bond ETF, especially since government notes tend to benefit from so-called flight-to-safety flows.
"As these fears have been stoked, whether it has been Russia and Ukraine or Israel and Hamas or Iraq, it
seems to me that there's been a bigger reaction in the government bond markets of the most safe-viewed
governments," says Rich Steinberg, a managing director and partner at HSW Advisors at HighTower in New
York.
But since interest rates on government bonds in places like the U.S. and Germany are already so low, there
isn't much hope for notable returns, he says. As such, he prefers high-quality, short-duration debt that offers a
little bit of yield, such as high-rated corporate debt.
-- Cash and Gold: The other go-to safety nets are cash and gold, though both have drawbacks.
Mr. Hepburn says his firm, which was building cash positions for part of the summer, prefers conservative
money-market funds such as those focused on U.S. government debt. "If stocks drop 10%, your
money-market fund has 10% created buying power when you go back into the stock [market]."
He suggests avoiding higher-yielding money-market funds, since some of them may invest in junk bonds.
Meanwhile, gold advocates say the metal should be a part of portfolios longer-term, although some experts
warn that gold doesn't always trade like a safe-harbor asset because supply and demand factors can
override geopolitical concerns.
But rather than viewing it as a temporary Band-Aid for global worries, Michael Cuggino, president and
portfolio manager of Permanent Portfolio Family of Funds, sees a longer-term case for gold: Central-bank
stimulus will ultimately lead to some inflation in the U.S. and elsewhere. "Over the long term, [gold] makes
sense for investors as an anchor or a counterweight for a lot of things that are going on."
-- Currencies: Paul Christopher, chief international strategist at Wells Fargo Advisors, suggests avoiding cash
because it has the biggest "opportunity cost," and he believes gold is headed lower. He recommends
investing in currencies such as the dollar, Swiss franc or yen, as hedges for geopolitical conflict since they
can appreciate and tend to be less volatile than, say, gold. Investors can use currency-focused ETFs for all
three. For instance, PowerShares DB US Dollar Index Bullish fund has returned 2.46% this year through
August, according to Lipper.

--- Page 105 ---
Page 105 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ultimately, investors should have a diversified investing plan in the first place that already includes these
kinds of assets, rather than rushing into gold or U.S. government bonds at the last minute, advisers say. With
"a diversified asset allocation that gives what you need to live, you can ride out that storm," says Mr.
Steinberg.

Investing in Funds & ETFS: A Monthly Analysis --- Spotlight: Land REITs: Time to Buy The Farm?
Investing in Funds & ETFS: A Monthly Analysis --- Spotlight: Land REITs: Time to Buy The Farm?
By Liz Moyer
324 words
8 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investing in farmland and forest tracts is sometimes described as "gold with a coupon" -- a hedge against
inflation, because land prices tend to rise along with overall inflation, that also pays a steady income.
Small investors have access to these markets through publicly traded real-estate investment trusts. Share
prices of REITs that buy farm and timber land have dropped in recent weeks, but most of them yield
dividends well above the current yields of about 0.95% on the three-year U.S. Treasury note and 2.11% on
the seven-year Treasury.
For farms, there are two relatively new REITs. Gladstone Land raised $57 million in its January 2013 initial
public offering and now owns 6,833 acres on 28 farms in California, Florida, Michigan, Oregon and Arizona,
according to securities filings. Its dividend yield was about 5.82% in recent trading. Farmland Partners,
meanwhile, had a $53 million initial public offering in April and owns 41 farms with 23,630 acres in Illinois,
Nebraska and Colorado, along with three grain-storage facilities. Its dividend yield is about 0.90%.
Publicly traded timberland REITs have been around longer. The two biggest are Weyerhaeuser and Plum
Creek Timber.
Weyerhaeuser converted to a REIT in 2010 and recently completed a spinoff of its home-building division. It
is one of the biggest forest-products companies, with seven million acres of timber in the U.S. Weyerhaeuser
recently raised its dividend to 29 cents a share from 22 cents; its yield is about 2.80%.
Plum Creek Timber owns 6.7 million acres in the Northwest, Northeast and South -- and six wood-product
conversion facilities in the Northwest. Its dividend yield is about 4.35%. Rounding out the biggest timberland
REITs are Potlatch Corp. and Rayonier, with dividend yields of about 3.20% and 5.78%, respectively.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 67.78, or 0.4%, to 17137.36, just shy of its July 16 record. The
Nasdaq Composite Index added 20.61, or 0.5%, to 2007.71.
Friday's gains capped a week of back-and-forth moves for U.S. stocks, as investors paused following a strong
August for the market and shifted their attention to Europe, where further economic-stimulus measures were
announced by the European Central Bank on Thursday.
Both the S&P 500 and the Dow rose 0.2% for the week.
The U.S. economy added 142,000 jobs in August, the Labor Department said Friday, falling well short of
expectations for an increase of 225,000 jobs.
Investors widely expect the Fed to raise interest rates next year following an October end to bond
purchases, pointing out that the economy continues to improve at a steady pace.
But the timing of any rate increase remains up in the air, and investors say the weaker-than-expected jobs
data give the central bank more leeway to lift rates later rather than sooner.
"The bottom line is that [the jobs report is] disappointing, but it's good for stocks, because rates stay lower,"
said James Swanson, chief investment strategist at MFS Investment Management, which oversees $429.7
billion.
"You're still in the sweet spot where the economy is expanding," Mr. Swanson said, adding that stocks should
continue to rise if the Fed increases rates next year, as expected.
In the futures market, traders pushed odds of a rate increase at the Fed's June 2015 meeting down to 45%,
compared with 51% a day earlier, according to data from CME Group. The calculations are based on prices
of 30-day fed-funds futures, the main derivative instrument for traders and investors betting on the Fed's
official-interest-rate outlook.
Buying in bonds initially took yields lower on the jobs report, with the 10-year U.S. Treasury note's yield falling
from 2.448% late Thursday. By Friday afternoon, however, bonds had shed their gains. The yield on the
10-year rebounded to 2.460%. Yields and price move in opposite directions.

"There was a view that [the jobs figure] could be a really strong number, and it came in a lot weaker than
expected," said Michael Fredericks, portfolio manager of BlackRock's $9 billion Multi-Asset Income fund. He
added that one report isn't enough "to change a growing consensus that the U.S. economy is pretty healthy

In corporate news, Family Dollar Stores Inc. said its board rejected Dollar General Corp.'s revised takeover

REVIEW --- Books: When Labor Was Capital --- Before the Civil War, slavery played a central role in the economies of both North and South
REVIEW --- Books: When Labor Was Capital --- Before the Civil War, slavery played a central role in
the economies of both North and South
By Fergus M. Bordewich
1,813 words
6 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Slavery's defenders often portrayed the South's "peculiar institution" as the antithesis of money-grubbing
Yankee capitalism, rooted in an idealized agrarian hierarchy of white master and enslaved African that had
been ordained for all time by God and natural law. Indeed, they often insisted that it was a supremely
charitable endeavor that saved the slave from his own innate barbarism, asserting, for example, that they had
to provide for elderly, infirm and immature slaves whether they were productive or not, as if they were
members of their own family -- albeit of a very inferior sort. As the wealthy South Carolina planter and
politician James H.
Hammond condescendingly put it in 1845, in a truculent rebuttal to attacks on slavery made by a British
abolitionist: "We must therefore content ourselves with our dear labor under the consoling reflection that what
is lost to us is gained to humanity." Abolitionists were contemptuous of such self-serving nonsense, but they
too tended to see slavery as an economically inefficient, and morally reprehensible, hangover from the
premodern past.
In "The Half Has Never Been Told," Edward E. Baptist takes passionate issue with such assumptions. He
asserts that slavery was neither inherently inefficient nor a counterpoint to capitalism. Rather, he says, it was
woven inextricably into the transnational fabric of early 19th-century capitalism. Banks and financiers fed it
with the investment it needed to continue expanding and were rewarded with handsome profits from the labor
of enslaved millions. Although crashes, depressions and market fluctuations inevitably affected the
slave-based economy, large-scale investors in slavery consistently earned handsome profits, at least in the
rich cotton-growing regions of the Deep South.
The morality of slavery rarely if ever entered into the business equation. As the number of slaves in the U.S.
swelled from just under one million at the dawn of the century to about four million at the time of the Civil War,
investors consistently demonstrated their confidence in slavery's profitability. As the historian Walter Johnson
has eloquently put it, slaves represented "a congealed form of the capital upon which the commercial
development of the [Mississippi River] Valley depended. . . . The cords of credit and debt -- of advance and
obligation -- that cinched the Atlantic economy together were anchored with the mutually defining values of
land and slaves: without land and slaves, there was no credit, and without slaves, land itself was valueless."
The value of the dollar, Mr. Johnson adds, "as often as not . . . turned out to be backed by flesh rather than
gold."
As early as the 1820s, says Mr. Baptist, slave owners commanded the biggest pool of collateral in the United
States: two million slaves worth more than $1 billion. "Not only was that almost 20 percent of all the wealth
owned by all US citizens," Mr. Baptist writes, "but it was the most liquid part of that wealth, thanks to the
efficiency of markets manned by professional slave traders." Slaves were a uniquely flexible commodity:
There was a ready market for them everywhere in the South; they could be either sold or leased; they could
be moved from place to place under their own power; and unlike tools and buildings, they naturally
reproduced, adding to the value of their master's investment.
As the native Indian tribes were removed from their land -- either by treaty or at gunpoint -- investment capital
gushed in. In just eight years, from 1824 to 1832, the Philadelphia-based Bank of the United States, the
banker for the federal government, multiplied the amount of its loans to Mississippi Valley slave owners 16
times over. By 1832, according to Mr. Baptist, at least one-third of all the bank's capital had been allocated to
planters, slave traders, merchants and local banks on the "slave frontier" of the southwestern states. The
depth of the bank's commitment, in turn, gave European investors the confidence to lavishly inject their own
currency into American slavery.
For example, the Consolidated Association of the Planters of Louisiana -- a local bank chartered in 1827 --
enjoyed a lucrative relationship with Baring Brothers of London, a firm that lobbied successfully to persuade
the Louisiana legislature to back the association's bonds with public credit. Thus if the association failed to

--- Page 110 ---
Page 110 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
pay off its bonds, Louisiana's taxpayers would be liable for the debt. Baring would eventually handle some
$2.5 million in bond sales for the association, marketing to clients in Britain as well as the European
continent.
Mr. Baptist writes most effectively about the cotton-growing states of the Mississippi River Valley. During the
boom years of the 1830s, the region transformed itself from a frontier backwater into the wealthiest and most
productive agricultural region in the United States. Andrew Jackson's termination of the Bank of the United
States, in 1836, created new opportunities for buccaneer financiers. A multitude of thinly capitalized and
virtually unregulated banks flooded the region with unbacked paper money. In Louisiana alone, the number of
such "mushroom banks" quadrupled from four to 16 in just a few years, while the total amount of capital
authorized by the state legislature exploded from $9 million to $46 million, giving rise to speculation that New
Orleans might soon become America's financial capital.
Remarked one observer in 1835: "People here are run mad with speculation. They do business in . . . a kind
of phrenzy." Mr. Baptist notes that since large-scale slave owners commonly included judges, politicians and
state officials -- that is, the same men who controlled banking policy and debt collection -- elite borrowers
were rarely foreclosed, even if they fell behind on payments: "As enslavers multiplied their leverage, they
multiplied their revenue without increasing their individual risk."
By 1837, the total amount of bank loans available to southwestern borrowers had grown to $80 million from
$40 million in 1832 -- one-third of the national total, Mr. Baptist says, and more than any other region of the
United States. Although investment was also made in infrastructure, such as railroads, Mr. Baptist maintains
that the major purpose of the loans was to expand the region's agriculture, which meant borrowing to
purchase the "tools of the trade" -- slaves and more slaves. Companies like Franklin & Armfield were ready to
meet the demand.
Mr. Baptist's fine-grained profile of Franklin & Armfield is sadly illuminating. The firm rode rising demand to
become the biggest slave-trading company in the United States, moving many thousands of "surplus" slaves
from the Upper South to the plantations of what was then called the "Southwest." To finance its activities,
Franklin & Armfield drew up to $40,000 at a time from the Bank of the United States to buy slaves for the
Mississippi Valley markets. According to Mr. Baptist, about 5% of all the commercial credit handled by the
bank in 1831-32 passed at some point through the hands of that single slave-trading company.
Although the vast majority of the company's "human stock" would wind up in the cotton fields, many females,
attractive mulattoes in particular, were destined for prostitution. In one of several letters that Mr. Baptist
quotes, a company senior partner suggests matter-of-factly that two women he had recently purchased "could
soon pay for themselves by keeping a whore house . . . for the Exclusive benefit of the concern and its allied
agents."
Considering his subject matter, Mr. Baptist writes with verve and a good eye for the dramatic. This virtue is
also the book's greatest shortcoming, however. In an effort to humanize the often forgotten slaves whose
lives were, after all, at the heart of the global economic forces that drove the cotton economy, he resorts too
often to novelistic devices that undermine the reader's patience and trust. Describing the execution of a
rebellious slave named Amar, Mr. Baptist writes: "In his head, as he slumped and fell, were 50 billion
neurons. They held the secrets of turning sugarcane sap into white crystals . . . they held the cunning with
which he sought out his lover's desires. . . . The dancing electrons in Amar's brain caressed forty-five years of
words, pictures, feelings, the village imam with his old book, his mother calling him from the door of a
mud-brick house." Elsewhere Mr. Baptist writes of an unnamed man en route to the slave market in New
Orleans: "He heard the women on the other deck crying for a dying baby or sister; heard them fight as the
sailors took them into the crew's quarters one by one, to be raped. He saw them drag out men who had gone
stiff and grinning. The angel's fingers clawed at him too." Is this slave a real person? A composite? Wholly
imagined? Mr. Baptist doesn't tell us.
Anyone averse to such squishy stuff will find a penetrating discussion of many the same topics in Walter
Johnson's often beautifully written (if sometimes confusingly organized) "River of Dark Dreams: Slavery and
Empire in the Cotton Kingdom" (2013). That work places antebellum slave-owning in a broad context of
proslavery ideology, Atlantic commodity markets and Southern schemes for global ascendancy. Like Mr.
Baptist, Mr. Johnson argues that abstracting slavery from industrial development in the U.S. -- or, for that
matter, in Britain -- is fundamentally ahistorical. Mississippi, he rightly says, does not have to look like
Manchester, England, or Lowell, Mass., to make it an engine of capitalism.
Slavery weathered the Panic of 1837, which ruined many of the "mushroom banks" and debtors who lacked
helpful friends in the local courthouse. In the 1850s, the slave-based economy experienced a dramatic
resurgence when a new wave of "Negro fever" doubled the price of slaves in relation to that of other goods.
On the cusp of the Civil War, slavery showed no sign of dying a natural death, except in parts of Maryland
and Delaware. Slavery remained, Mr. Baptist says, "both modernizing and modern" and its growth "muscular,
dynamic."

--- Page 111 ---
Page 111 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In his January 1861 State of the Union address, the pro-Southern president, James Buchanan, spent less
time addressing the secession crisis in South Carolina than he did expressing his hope of acquiring Cuba for
the United States -- a longtime goal of slave owners and investors who saw it as the best opportunity for
extending the reach of American slavery. Only civil war and hundreds of thousands of lives would finally put
an end to the lucrative partnership between the cruel machine of Southern slavery and the roaring engines of
capitalism.
---
Mr. Bordewich's most recent book is "America's Great Debate: Henry Clay, Stephen A. Douglas, and the
Compromise That Preserved the Union."
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: American Slavery Wasn't Capitalist" -- WSJ Sep 16, 2014)

S&P 500 Declines For Third Session
Thursday's Markets
S&P 500 Declines For Third Session
By Chris Dieterich and Saumya Vaishampayan
574 words
5 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks failed to hold early gains fueled by a surprise interest-rate cut from the European Central Bank,
leaving the S&P 500 with its third loss in a row.
The S&P 500 dropped 3.07 points, or 0.2%, to 1997.65. The Dow industrials fell 8.70 points, or 0.1%, to
17069.58 after hitting an intraday record of 17161.55. The Nasdaq Composite Index declined 10.28 points, or
0.2%, to 4562.29.
While the session marked the third consecutive day the S&P 500 has ended in the red, losses have been
small, totaling just 5.7 points, or 0.3%.
Energy stocks in the S&P 500 fell 1.3%, by far the session's worst performers, after a U.S. judge ruled that oil
producer BP PLC was grossly negligent in the 2010 Deepwater Horizon rig explosion and oil spill. BP
tumbled $2.82, or 5.9%, to $44.89 in New York trading.
European stocks jumped after the ECB unexpectedly lowered its interest rates to all-time lows and President
Mario Draghi announced two new programs under which the central bank will buy asset-backed securities
and covered bonds issued by eurozone banks.
That news pushed the Stoxx Europe 600 index up 1.1% and Germany's DAX up 1%.
The cheer spilled over into Japan early Friday, as the Nikkei rose 0.6%, with the yen hitting its weakest level
against the dollar in nearly six years.
Initially U.S. stocks moved higher Thursday, but the rally ran out of steam. Traders said investors were
reluctant to make big moves ahead of Friday's August employment report.
A stronger-than-forecast jobs number could provide Fed officials with evidence that the economy has
improved enough to support higher interest rates, which some fear could usher in market volatility. Signs of
improving growth, rising corporate profits and expectations for easy-money from the Fed have helped drive
the S&P 500 up 8.1% in 2014.
On Friday, the Labor Department is expected to report that the U.S. economy added 225,000 nonfarm
payrolls in August. The unemployment rate is expected to tick lower to 6.1% from 6.2% a month earlier.
Rick Fier, director of execution at Conifer Securities in New York, said that many of his firm's hedge-fund
clients sought protective positions headed into Thursday's ECB meeting and left those bearish-leaning trades
intact ahead of Friday's jobs report.
"There's nervousness," Mr. Fier said. "People have been trimming and trimming and trimming" their winning
stock bets this week, he said.
Still, underlying sentiment toward U.S. stocks is generally positive.
Yousef Abassi, a market strategist at brokerage JonesTrading Institutional Services, said that while stocks
have paused in recent days, he sees few obstacles to derail the rally, with many large investors willing to buy
stocks. "It doesn't even feel like down is an option" beyond a shallow drop, he said, citing the market's recent
"immunity" from global flare-ups.
In the bond market, the yield on the benchmark 10-year note rose to 2.448% from 2.409% late Wednesday.
In commodity markets, crude-oil futures fell 1.1% to settle at $94.45 a barrel. Gold futures fell 0.3% to
$1,265.10 a troy ounce.

Most Stocks Start September With a Step Back
By Saumya Vaishampayan

3 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks ended mostly lower, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average and S&P 500 pulling back from
advances in August that marked the largest monthly gains since February.
The Dow fell 30.89 points, or 0.2%, to 17067.56, and the S&P 500 shed 1.09 points, or less than 0.1%, to
2002.28. However, the Nasdaq Composite Index rose 17.92, or 0.4%, to 4598.19.
"We've had a nice run here in the last few weeks," said Andy Brooks, head of U.S. equity trading at T. Rowe
Price. "It's not surprising the market has given a little back."
Stocks remained near records. The S&P 500 is off just 0.05% from its Friday record close and the Dow is off
0.4% from its record reached on July 16. August's monthly gains marked the 10th in the past 12 months for
both indexes.
Many investors said the backdrop for stocks remains positive, as improving economic data and low yields on
U.S. Treasurys could continue to boost appetite for stocks. "Every single reasonable-sized panic selloff. . .has
been met with a lot of buying," said Viren Chandrasoma, managing director of equity trading at Credit Suisse.
The energy sector was the worst performer on the S&P 500 on Tuesday, losing 1.3%. Chevron fell $1.91, or
1.5%, to $127.54, and Exxon Mobil declined 97 cents, or 1%, to 98.49.
Losses came on the first trading day of September, which has historically been a bad month for stocks.
"Maybe September will be true to form and be a month to make people nervous," said Chris Bertelsen, chief
investment officer at Global Financial Private Capital. Still, he said he is viewing pullbacks as buying
opportunities.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note rose to 2.418%, from 2.347% on Friday. Yields move inversely to
prices. The 10-year yield remains near its lowest level since June 2013.
A rally in the dollar weighed on dollar-denominated commodities, which become more expensive as the dollar
gains for buyers using other currencies. Crude-oil prices fell 3.2%, to $92.88 a barrel, and gold shed 1.7%, to
$1,263.70 a troy ounce.
Investors looked ahead to monetary-policy decisions from major central banks that could bring additional
stimulus and the U.S.'s monthly jobs report. Recent lackluster economic data have added to expectations that
the European Central Bank will take action this week. Activity in the eurozone's manufacturing sector slowed
more sharply than first estimated in August, data showed Monday. The Stoxx Europe 600 fell less than 0.1%.
While U.S. stocks have largely been buffered from geopolitical risks in Ukraine and the Mideast, a continued
downturn in the eurozone could hurt U.S. multinational companies, said Diane Jaffee, who oversees about
$7.5 billion in relative value strategies as a senior portfolio manager at TCW Group Inc. The announcement of
further stimulus measures would be positive for those companies, she said.
In corporate news, Home Depot fell 1.88, or 2%, to 91.15. The home-improvement company is investigating a
potential breach of credit- or debit-card data.

Metals Crumple As Dollar Advances
Commodities Report
Metals Crumple As Dollar Advances
By Tatyana Shumsky
454 words
3 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold prices fell to a two-month low and palladium posted its steepest decline in 2 1/2 months, as pressure
from a stronger dollar snuffed out investor appetite for the haven assets.
Gold for December delivery, the most actively traded contract, fell $22.40, or 1.7%, to $1,265 a troy ounce,
the lowest closing price since June 11 on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
The dollar gained on investors' expectations of stimulus efforts by the European Central Bank after its chief,
Mario Draghi, signaled that further accommodative steps are necessary to revive the region's economy.
"The prospect for the ECB to be easing means a weaker euro and it lends the dollar strength," said Frank
Lesh, a broker and futures analyst with FuturePath Trading LLC in Chicago.
The greenback's strength made dollar-denominated gold more expensive for buyers who use other
currencies to fund their purchases. December gold fell as low as $1,263.10 an ounce on Tuesday.
The shift in currency markets comes as the U.S. nears the end of its stimulus efforts and moves closer toward
raising interest rates, developments that are likely to further boost the dollar, said Adam Klopfenstein, a
senior market strategist with Archer Financial Services LLC in Chicago.
"The U.S. is doing better than its peers, whereas in Europe they're just beginning on this path of stimulus," he
said.
Palladium futures pulled back from a 13-year high as some investors locked in gains on the recent rally, while
others saw the stronger dollar as a threat to further gains. Futures topped $900 an ounce Friday amid
escalating tensions between Russia and the West over Moscow's role in destabilizing Ukraine.
"Palladium was bid up on thoughts that the Ukraine and Russia situation would bring about some kind of
disruption, but we're not seeing that yet. Russia isn't retaliating against the sanctions with any cessation of
metals exports," Mr. Lesh said.
Russia is the world's top palladium producer, with roughly 40% of supply, and some traders worry that
Western trade sanctions or retaliatory trade curbs by the Kremlin could affect the palladium market. Palladium
is primarily used in car-exhaust filters, where it helps break down pollutants.
In other markets, platinum prices tumbled 1.1% to their lowest close since April, as futures moved in
sympathy with other precious metals. Some traders buy and sell precious metals as a basket, and losses in
one market can prompt these traders to cut back holdings across the board.
Nymex platinum for October delivery fell $15.80, or 1.1%, to $1,408.90 a troy ounce.
