Back to Work: This Autumn's Business Landscape --- Challenges Include Filling Jobs, Inspiring Consumers, Revving Detroit's Engine
Back to Work: This Autumn's Business Landscape --- Challenges Include Filling Jobs, Inspiring
Consumers, Revving Detroit's Engine
2,523 words
2 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Part one of The Wall Street Journal's annual fall business outlook. Return Wednesday to read what's ahead
in the film, advertising, manufacturing and health-insurance industries.]
Summer brought companies hopeful signs of economic recovery. This fall, the optimism will be put to the test.
That is because it is unclear whether consumers -- the engine behind more than two-thirds of the American
economy -- are ready to resume spending at significant levels.
Signals are mixed. After years of lackluster growth, corporate revenue for large companies grew more rapidly
in the second quarter, and company forecasts suggest that trend will continue in the second half. The
Conference Board said consumer confidence picked up in August.
Employment continued to tick upward. Gross domestic product jumped in the second quarter as businesses
and consumers made up for the first quarter's lousy weather. Economists are predicting growth at a 3% rate
for the second half and 2% for the full year.
Still, the retail industry, tied more closely to consumer spending than any other, is struggling. The Commerce
Department said U.S. retail spending was flat in July. And household spending declined 0.1% in July, the first
drop in personal spending since January.
Investors are clearly nervous as well. For much of August, retailers and a grocer made up most of the 10
worst-performing stocks in the S&P 500 index: Coach Inc . was down 34% for the year; Whole Foods Market
Inc ., down 32%; Mattel Inc ., down 28%; Staples Inc ., down 26%; and Bed Bath & Beyond Inc . and Best
Buy Co ., each down about 20%. The Dow Jones U.S. Retail Index has struggled to mark gains so far this
year.
On Aug. 26, Best Buy predicted a tough holiday season marked by steep discounts and weak demand after
recording a 2% drop in sales at U.S. stores open at least 14 months. And in some sectors, such as low-end
dollar stores, companies may be benefiting more from market share than broader growth.
"Revenues are doing better than they were," says Howard Silverblatt, senior index analyst at S&P Dow Jones
Indices. But companies "don't think consumers are going to be spending that much more."
At Home Corp ., a closely held Texas-based home-furnishings chain with 75 locations in 21 states, says sales
are up 20% from a year earlier. The company, which advertises low prices, is seeing new customers,
more-frequent visits and higher ticket prices amid mediocre increases in overall consumer spending, which
means much of the growth is coming at the expense of competitors, Chief Executive Lee Bird says.
"There's growth here, but we're getting a disproportionate share of that," Mr. Bird says. "We're definitely
taking share while growing with a sluggish economy."
And then there is the question of how much consumers have to spend. What isn't spent is generally saved.
But the savings rate in the U.S. is about 5.3% -- at or below recent trends.
In other words, there may not be enough untapped spending power sitting on the sidelines, waiting for
confidence to improve, to fuel a strong consumer recovery, says Dean Baker, an economist and co-director of
the Center for Economic and Policy Research in Washington.
So far, that has been true for customers of Hibbett Sports Inc ., a sporting-goods retailer with primarily
lower-income customers in the South, Southwest, Midwest and mid-Atlantic, says Scott Bowman , the
company's chief financial officer. Hibbett sales inched up just 0.1% in the quarter ended Aug. 2, excluding
new and closed stores.

--- Page 117 ---
Page 117 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"That lower-than-average-income consumer has a few more headwinds than the upper-tier consumer, with
static wage growth, underemployment," Mr. Bowman says. "I think it will get better, but it will be gradual."
Some economists are more upbeat. While summer monthly sales figures looked grim, the year so far hasn't
been terrible. At about 4%, year-over-year retail sales growth is reasonable, says Mark Zandi, chief
economist for Moody's Analytics. Consumer spending overall also roughly matches income growth, with
consumer confidence at prerecession levels and debt payments at record lows, he says.
"As long as the job market continues to improve, and it is, then I think consumers will continue to ramp up
their spending consistent with that," or possibly higher if they decide it is safe to borrow more, Mr. Zandi says.
"I think it is fair to say that consumers are doing their part for the recovery."
-- Theo Francis
HIRING
It is getting tougher for companies to say, "You're hired."
U.S. employers are taking longer -- 25 working days, on average -- to fill vacant positions. That is a 13-year
high, according to the Dice-DFH Vacancy Duration Measure, an index created by University of Chicago
economist Steven Davis . At companies with 5,000 or more workers, the time to hire is even longer, at 58.1
working days. The index defines working days as Monday through Saturday.
Economists and employers say there is no single cause for the slow hiring pace, but the lag time can be read
as a proxy for corporate confidence in the economy. On one hand, companies are feeling sunny enough to
post jobs -- openings reached 4.7 million in June, the highest number since 2001. But fearful that the
economy could falter, they are finding it hard to commit to hires.
Typically, a longer time between employers advertising a job and having an offer accepted is a sign of a
thriving economy, suggesting there are more openings than job seekers to fill them. But with nearly 10 million
Americans currently unemployed, that doesn't describe today's labor market.
Employers have become "extremely picky," which also partially accounts for stagnant wage growth, Mr. Davis
says. "If employers were more confident, they'd bid up wages, and that hasn't happened."
Employers have raised the credentials required for jobs, according to recent studies, and some have
intensified prehire screenings. As a result, bosses are having trouble finding candidates who not only possess
the skills for the job but also clear those higher hurdles.
Also potentially slowing the process are social networks such as LinkedIn, where employers court so-called
passive candidates who are already employed at competitors and not officially looking for a new job. While
such networks expand the pool of potential hires, wooing those candidates takes longer than plucking
someone actively searching for work, says Mr. Davis, who uses Labor Department data for the time-to-hire
index.
Thinner staffing in human-resources and recruiting departments may be another factor, since recruiters are
taking on a larger workload as employers post jobs. "Depending on how many hiring managers [company
recruiters are] dealing with, it's impossible" to fill jobs quickly, says Mark Mehler, co-founder of staffing
strategy consulting firm CareerXroads.
Bridgestone Retail Operations LLC , a division of tire maker Bridgestone Corp ., takes 21 to 30 days
(counting all days of the week) to hire an experienced mechanic or salesperson. In 2008, Bridgestone Retail
made those hires in about half that time.
Like many other employers, Bridgestone Retail , which is based in Bloomingdale, Ill., is conducting more
background, drug and physical screenings for candidates and has a more rigorous interview process for
white-collar hires.
That comes with a business cost. Bridgestone Retail Chairman and President Stu Crum says desirable
candidates sometimes go elsewhere rather than wait, and meanwhile, short-staffed stores may have to turn
customers away, or incur overtime costs for mechanics.
The company is trying out an outside firm to conduct background checks and sift applications in an effort to
speed hiring. Mr. Crum has set a target of 10 to 14 days for store staff and around 30 days for corporate
positions.
Even as he looks to speed up hiring, Mr. Crum wants to make sure the process doesn't get too easy. Getting
the right people into the company is the most important thing, he says, and the added rigor in hiring helps.

--- Page 118 ---
Page 118 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"When there's a larger pool out there you can make mistakes and there's another one standing in the queue,"
he says. Now, "when you hire someone you want to make sure they're the right one."
-- Lauren Weber and Rachel Feintzeig
AUTOS
Auto-industry executives are under pressure to make hay while the sun shines because there is no telling
how much longer the recovery from the industry's 2009 collapse has to run.
The climate now is as favorable for auto makers as it has been in years. Interest rates remain unusually low,
oil prices are remarkably stable and consumers in the world's two largest car markets -- the U.S. and China --
are snapping up sport-utility vehicles, luxury cars and, in the U.S., big pickup trucks, the most profitable
vehicles most car makers sell.
Even Europe has emerged from the worst of its funk, allowing mass market car makers such as Ford Motor
Co . and General Motors Co . to narrow losses.
Can it get any better? Probably not. Industry veterans know that fat years are inevitably followed by lean
years. Now is no time to kick back.
Auto makers used to respond to prosperity by splashing out on high-price acquisitions and exotic cars
developed more to boost executive egos than consumer demand. This time around, memories of recent
near-death appear to be curbing those impulses.
Still, there are critical calls industry executives have to get right while times are good.
High on the to-do list is judging whether the U.S. auto market's recovery is approaching its peak. Sales of
cars and light trucks in July ran at an annual pace of 16.5 million vehicles -- good compared with the 15.8
million a year earlier, but less good viewed against the 16.7 million of May and 16.9 million of June, according
to market researcher Autodata Corp . The cost of sales incentives is rising, Autodata says.
Few industry executives and forecasters expect another severe crash in demand. But auto demand and auto
makers' profits are highly sensitive to interest rates and relatively small shifts in supply and demand. It is
likely U.S. interest rates will rise, possibly next year, increasing the costs of the zero-interest loans car
makers are using to keep the party rolling. More factory building in the U.S. -- and China -- won't help keep
prices high. That means curbing costs will be critical.
Industry executives must also home in on the risks in emerging markets, which collectively account for more
than half of world-wide auto sales and will be close to 60% by decade's end, according to IHS Inc .
The past few months have hit auto makers with reminders of how volatile developing markets can be. Three
of the four of the so-called BRIC markets -- Brazil, Russia and India -- have suffered downdrafts in demand,
aggravated in Russia by blowback from the conflict in Ukraine.
China, the fourth BRIC country, continues to grow, but foreign auto makers have been stung as government
regulators have attacked them for allegedly fixing prices for repair parts and demanding inflated prices for
vehicles.
When auto executives aren't worrying about Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen or Chinese antitrust
authorities, they can fret about whether someone in Silicon Valley is about to monkey-wrench their business
plans.
Regulators want car makers to roughly double the average fuel efficiency of new cars sold in the U.S. by
2025 to cut down on greenhouse gas emissions. There is also increasing interest in equipping cars with
advanced safety technology and autonomous driving capability that can help drivers avoid accidents, rather
than just survive them.
Those two trends are combining to make cars increasingly digital, with more of their value coming from
computing power and software (the better to manage fuel consumption and self-driving systems) and features
that rely on being connected to the Internet.
Auto makers and Silicon Valley technologists have struggled for years to mesh their cultures. The ability to
collaborate with Silicon Valley's digital technologists -- and cope with what Silicon Valley entrepreneurs like
Tesla Motors Inc . CEO Elon Musk are doing to disrupt traditional ways of buying or owning cars -- will be
critical to auto-business success.
-- Joseph B. White

--- Page 119 ---
Page 119 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
ENERGY
Everyone knows the U.S. is now pumping oil and gas in new places from North Dakota to Alabama. Less
known is that Texas remains the powerhouse when it comes to oil output -- and may be on the verge of
producing a glut.
One of the state's oldest oil fields, the Permian Basin in West Texas, is booming again, thanks to advanced
technologies such as hydraulic fracturing and horizontal drilling. And Permian oil output shows no signs of
stopping at its current 1.7 million barrels a day.
"You're definitely going to hit the 2 million barrel mark," Bob Reeves , chief executive of Athlon Energy Inc.,
which is based in Fort Worth, Texas, said at an investor conference recently. "I think it's not a matter of if, it's
just a matter of when." Production could ultimately reach 3 million barrels a day, he said.
The Bakken oil field in North Dakota pumps about 1 million barrels a day now. The Eagle Ford in South
Texas: 1.5 million.
The oil flowing from the Permian is so plentiful that it is threatening prices. A barrel of oil in Midland, Texas,
the Permian's trading hub, cost $78.97 in mid-August. In Cushing, Okla., the national storage and
transportation center for oil, the same crude fetched $96.48 -- $17.51 more.
Last year, the Midland discount averaged only $1.75, roughly the cost of transporting the oil to Cushing.
Basically, the Permian is producing more oil than pipelines can handle. Count on the next couple of years
being full of pipeline planning, construction and community fights. Trains full of crude also will continue to
rumble out of towns like Wink, Texas, to California and the Gulf Coast.
That is good news for refiners, who can take advantage of inexpensive crude. But it doesn't translate into
lower gasoline prices for consumers, who are subject to the wider global market. And companies drilling for
oil will face pressure to their bottom lines.
The Midland-Cushing gap narrowed at the end of August when a pipeline said it would reverse direction,
moving oil out of the Permian Basin to Oklahoma. But as Permian Basin oil output increases, price
disruptions are liable to emerge again.
Barring a significant drop in global oil prices, Permian drilling is set to accelerate. Companies active in West
Texas are testing a dozen rock strata that can produce significant amounts of oil.
The challenge for drillers no longer is finding and pumping oil. It is access to markets -- through new pipelines
and political pressure so they can export Texas tea to the rest of the world.
-- Russell Gold

Health & Wellness -- What's Your Workout? For Pam Shriver, Children Put a New Spin on Tennis
Health & Wellness -- What's Your Workout? For Pam Shriver, Children Put a New Spin on Tennis
By Jen Murphy
1,213 words
2 September 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
D3
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Pam Shriver's fiercest competitors used to be Chris Evert, Martina Navratilova and Steffi Graf. But these
days, the former tennis professional and ESPN broadcaster considers her 10-year-old son her toughest
opponent.
After she played her last match in 1997, Ms. Shriver barely picked up a racket, despite having a tennis court
in the backyard of her Los Angeles home. "When I retired, I discovered golf," she says.
Ms. Shriver, 52, won 133 titles during her 19-year tennis career, including 22 Grand Slam doubles titles, and
an Olympic gold medal in women's doubles at the 1988 Olympic Games in Seoul.
Ms. Shriver, who is reporting at the U.S. Open at in Flushing, N.Y., says it wasn't until two years ago, when
her three children started getting interested in tennis, that she found herself back on the court. "Suddenly I
was hitting almost daily," she says.
Ms. Shriver has 8-year-old twins, a girl and boy, and a 10-year-old son who plays in a youth tennis league.
"My 10-year-old can hit well enough to give me a real workout," she says. "We're not grinding out hours on
the court. But about 20 minutes a day we hit the ball and have fun."
A lifetime of pounding tennis balls across the court has left her with lower-back pain and a weak right
shoulder, which has required several surgeries. She says devoting 40 minutes a day to her gym workouts has
allowed her to keep up with her children. Strength training has helped keep her shoulder pain in check, while
golf has helped her back pain, she says.
"When I first started playing golf I didn't consider it a real workout," says Ms. Shriver. "Anything that didn't
keep your heart rate at 180 to 200 beats a minute for a sustained period of time wasn't a workout in my
macho tennis-player mind."
She says she's changed her tune. "I find golf keeps my spine more flexible and if I walk the whole course, it
takes four-plus hours," she says.
Ms. Shriver has found other ways to keep fit via her children. In the fall, she referees their youth soccer
games, and this year she coached her older son's basketball team.
"Being a mom, you want your kids to be active and that actually got me playing sports again," she says.
The Workout
Ms. Shriver's gym is at the Brentwood Country Club, which is a seven-minute drive from her house, and she
often gets her workouts in while the children are at school. She does 20 to 30 minutes of cardio on the
elliptical and treadmill. She spends 15 to 20 minutes doing two sets of eight to 10 reps on eight weight
machines. She focuses on strengthening her shoulders.
A strength session might include lat pulldowns, triceps extensions, biceps curls, bench presses, seated rows,
squats, leg extensions and hamstring curls. Ms. Shriver uses resistance tubing to strengthen the joints
around her shoulders. She always devotes five to 10 minutes a day to core work.
She travels about 60 days a year for ESPN to cover the U.S. Open, the Australian Open, the French Open
and Wimbledon. "I work out just as much on the road as I do at home. I don't primp," she says, "so I can fit in
20 minutes of cardio at the hotel gym before I head off" to the courts.
Ms. Shriver is on the tennis court almost daily with her children when she is home. She says a full round of
golf only happens 12 times a year, usually when she plays tournaments with the Brentwood Country Club's

--- Page 123 ---
Page 123 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
women golf team. "Getting in a full 18 holes of golf when you have three kids isn't exercise, it's a luxury," she
says.
The Diet
For breakfast Ms. Shriver has Greek yogurt with fruit, or when she is taking the children to school, she'll make
peanut butter and jelly on omega-three grain bread to eat on the go.
"If I don't have protein in the morning I don't feel right," she says.
She eats a lot of salads and vegetables and avoids carbs at night. Dinner is often chicken or grain-fed beef.
"My kids aren't big fish eaters, so if I eat out, I always order fish with a salad," she says.
The Gear
Ms. Shriver buys New Balance sneakers. "I wear a size 12 and they make a women's 12 and my orthotics fit
nicely in them," she says.
She has a collection of cotton T-shirts from past tennis competitions, such as the 1991 Pan American Games
in Havana, Cuba. "When I look in the mirror at the gym it's like doing a little flashback to events or trips of my
past."
She also likes Under Armour clothing. Later in her tennis career, she used a Yonex racket and still gets a few
free rackets, even though she isn't paid by the brand.
The Playlist
Ms. Shriver says she doesn't like to have the news on in front of the children, so she often watches it while
working out. "What's happening in the world makes you feel bad and working out makes you feel good, so it
balances out," she says.
---
Net Return: What It Takes to Get Back Into the Game
It takes full-body fitness, not just upper-body strength to deliver an ace serve and killer backhand.
"To play at the top of your game and avoid injury, you need a stable platform," says John DiFiori, the
physician in charge of all 23 sports teams at the University of California, Los Angeles. That means
strengthening the core, lower back, upper back, hip flexors and hamstrings, says Mr. DiFiori, who doesn't
work with Pam Shriver.
"Abdominal work such as crunches or exercises like throwing a medicine ball against the wall and using a
stability ball to perform lunges or squats will help build a strong core," he says.
While shoulder strength is important, he says it's just as important to strengthen the scapular stabilizers,
muscles that keep the shoulder blade and joint stable. "A lot of people make the mistake of just focusing on
strengthening the shoulder, but if you strengthen everything around it, the risk of overuse is reduced," Mr.
DiFiori says. He suggests performing exercises such as lat pulldowns, push-ups and shoulder shrugs.
Injuries are common when tennis players take time off and then jump into the game again, he says. Getting
back to the game "has to be gradual," he says, to allow the body to "recondition itself to accommodate the
stress the game puts on the shoulder, elbows, wrist and other muscles and joints."
One way to prevent injury is to do a pre-match warm-up. "Start by warming up the body with jogging, dynamic
stretches such as walking lunges and easy volleys," he says.
After play, Mr. DiFiori says a cooldown with some light stretching is just as important.
-- Jen Murphy

Stocks, Treasurys Rallied in August --- Global Appeal of U.S. Assets Lifts Dow, 10-Year Note to Biggest Monthly Gains Since Early in Year
Friday's Markets
Stocks, Treasurys Rallied in August --- Global Appeal of U.S. Assets Lifts Dow, 10-Year Note to
Biggest Monthly Gains Since Early in Year
By Matt Jarzemsky and Min Zeng
826 words
30 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J

English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks rallied Friday, fueling the Dow Jones Industrial Average's biggest monthly gain since February and
capping a month that featured vigorous rallies in both stocks and ultrasafe government bonds.
The action underscores the puzzle confronting investors heading into the long Labor Day weekend. Many
analysts say falling yields on U.S. Treasurys and equivalent European sovereign debt strengthen the case for
buying stocks, by highlighting the scarcity of steady investment income.
Others warn that falling yields are a warning sign for economic weakness, especially in Europe, where the
European Central Bank is expected in coming weeks to follow in the footsteps of the Federal Reserve and
adopt a bond-purchase program, in a bid to bolster flagging economic activity. The softness could presage a
pullback in stock prices, some investors warn.
Lately, the Dow and Treasurys alike have benefited from investors' perception that the U.S. economy
represents a better bet than growth-challenged developed nations, including much of Europe and Japan, or
sputtering emerging markets like China and Brazil. Adding to the appeal of U.S. assets: the perception that
the economic and financial-markets pictures here are isolated to some degree from unrest roiling Ukraine and
the Middle East.
"The U.S. economy is fine, and when you look at all the unrest elsewhere in the world, that just to me shows
how attractive Treasury securities could be," said Margie Patel, who oversees about $1.4 billion in stocks and
bonds as a portfolio manager at Wells Capital Management.
Still, she prefers equities, predicting continued corporate-profit growth and low interest rates that will likely
limit bonds' returns. "Growth looks relatively modest, but no matter how you look at the equity market it really
doesn't look overvalued."
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rallied 3.2% in August. On Friday, the blue-chip benchmark added 18.88
points, or 0.1%, to 17098.45.
The 10-year U.S. Treasury note this month posted its strongest price gains since January. The note's yield,
which falls as prices rise, finished Friday at 2.347% after hitting a 14-month low the previous session.
With stocks at records, many investors predict relatively modest gains from here, compared with a rally of
30% by the S&P 500 in 2013. Those gains have made bargains in the stock market scarcer, analysts say.
The S&P 500 rose 6.63 points, or 0.3%, Friday to a record 2003.37. It rose 3.8% in August. The Nasdaq
Composite Index rose 22.58, or 0.5%, Friday to 4580.27, for a 4.8% August gain.
Relatively risky corners of the stock market have performed even better recently. The Nasdaq Biotechnology
Index, which features many companies that are either unprofitable or richly valued due to lofty investor
expectations for their growth, added 1% Friday to cap a 10% monthly gain.
Europe is struggling to prevent low inflation from further crimping its already-weak economic recovery.
Brazil's statistics agency reported Friday the country's economy contracted 0.9% in the second quarter,
underscoring heightened concerns about emerging-markets growth.
"The overall bid for global fixed income, whether on the back of weaker euro-zone data, a dovish European
Central Bank or geopolitical concerns, are boosting Treasurys," said Stanley Sun, interest-rates strategist at
Nomura Securities International in New York. U.S. bonds are "attractive" compared to lower-yielding German
sovereign bonds, he said.

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Corporate bonds sold by lower-rated U.S. firms, or junk bonds, posted a total return of 1.56% in August
through Thursday, reflecting price gains and interest payments. U.S. investment-grade corporate bonds
returned 1.49% and U.S. municipal bonds returned 1.15%, according to data from Barclays PLC.
All of the returns outpaced the gain of 1.06% from the overall U.S. Treasury debt market.
"Global events have forced the safe-haven trade, and its continuation has forced investors to reach for yield,"
said Kevin Giddis, head of fixed income at Raymond James in Memphis, Tenn.

company's quarterly profit beat analysts' projections.

"As earnings season fades and people go on vacation . . . it makes sense that it's slow," said Bill Nichols,
head of U.S. equities at brokerage firm Cantor Fitzgerald.

Low Reserves Weigh On Argentina's Peso
Currency Trading
Low Reserves Weigh On Argentina's Peso
By Ken Parks
1,002 words
28 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BUENOS AIRES -- Argentina's international reserves are starting to dwindle in the wake of the country's
second sovereign-debt default in almost 13 years, putting added stress on the peso and an economy
believed to be in recession.
The default has hurt a country already racked by stagflation, the rare but toxic combination of weak economic
growth and high inflation. Argentina's reserves are falling due to capital flight, debt payments and
currency-market intervention, spurring fears the country eventually will run out of the cash it needs to pay
creditors and buy imports, a situation that could bring the economy grinding to a halt.
The Argentine peso, traded on a tightly regulated official market, has slipped from 8.20 per dollar before the
July 30 default to 8.4010 at Wednesday's close, its biggest slide since a devaluation of the currency early this
year. The decline came even as the central bank's stockpile of foreign currencies and gold fell $353 million
in the past week to a two-month low of $28.6 billion.
The decline in the peso traded on the unregulated black market has been steeper, from 12.30 per dollar in
late July to 14.42 per dollar on Wednesday, according to local websites that track the market. The growing
gap between the official rate and the black-market rate usually signals that ordinary Argentines expect further
depreciation of the official rate.
Argentines now joke that their peso has gone this year from the Dolar Messi to the Dolar Mascherano -- a
reference to the number 10 worn by Argentine soccer superstar Lionel Messi to the number 14 worn by the
standout midfielder Javier Mascherano.
The situation, which has President Cristina Kirchner and her ruling Peronists under fire, highlights the
increasingly difficult policy choices her administration faces ahead of next year's presidential elections. Term
limits bar Mrs. Kirchner from running again.
Mrs. Kirchner's cabinet chief, Jorge Capitanich, attributed the recent decline in reserves to debt payments,
including $642 million for the Paris Club of creditor nations, a drop in soy and gold prices, and fuel imports.
But analysts say at least part of the decline in reserves is due to the sale of dollars by the central bank to try
to prop up the peso. The bank has said it sold dollars in three of the past five trading sessions.
Most analysts expect the pressure on reserves to build as the year drags on due to debt payments and
waning dollar inflows from seasonal soy exports, which peak during spring and summer months. Investment
bank Jefferies LLC thinks Argentina's reserves will end the year between $22 billion and $25 billion, while
French bank BNP Paribas recently lowered its forecast to $21.6 billion from $23.6 billion.
The central bank also added to the uncertainty by trimming a benchmark rate a full percentage point to 26%
-- well below the country's estimated inflation rate of 40% -- just two weeks after the default. The move
heightened pressure on the peso since Argentines grew convinced the government was more interested in
priming the economy than controlling inflation or the peso.
On Tuesday, the bank raised the rate back to 27%. In a bid to protect reserves, the bank also severely
restricted the amount of dollars it makes available to importers, according to people familiar with the matter.
The bank couldn't immediately be reached for comment.
"You will see more controls . . . on imports and [capital] flows in order to maintain reserve stocks. That
obviously has an impact on growth. In essence, what you are seeing is a government that is willing to trade
growth for reserves," said Sebastian Vargas, an economist at Barclays.

--- Page 128 ---
Page 128 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the past month, more Argentines have been trying to swap their pesos for dollars, taking advantage of
rules that allow taxpayers to legally buy limited amounts of greenbacks. Those purchases are at $239 million
so far this month, surpassing the $205 million for all of July.
"The central bank needs to find a combination of a weaker [official] exchange rate and higher domestic rates
to take some pressure off the demand for dollars," says Alejo Costa, head of fixed-income strategy at Buenos
Aires-based brokerage and investment-banking firm Puente. Benchmark interest rates probably need to be
at least three percentage points below inflation to give savers enough of an incentive to park their money in
pesos, Mr. Costa said.
Up until the default, which stems from a legal fight between Buenos Aires and creditors still owed from the
country's 2001 default, investors were optimistic that Argentina would resolve long-standing creditor disputes
to borrow the money it needs from international credit markets to stabilize the economy. Argentina's economy
contracted slightly in the first quarter compared with the year-ago period, according to government figures.
Economists expect the economy to shrink about 1% this year.
Earlier this year, Argentina agreed to pay $9.7 billion to the Paris Club of creditor nations and gave Spanish
oil company Repsol SA about $5 billion in bonds as compensation for seizing its Argentine subsidiary.
Common wisdom held that Mrs. Kirchner would quickly do the same with a small group of hedge funds suing
Argentina in U.S. courts to collect on debt the country repudiated in 2001.
However, Argentina defaulted on $539 million of interest payments due July 30, when the judge presiding
over the case blocked the distribution of that money to restructured bondholders after the Kirchner
administration refused to pay $1.6 billion to the hedge funds.
Argentina now faces more claims on its shrinking stockpile of dollars from the Paris Club and Repsol deals,
but still can't borrow abroad to bolster its reserves because of the default.
"The macro imbalances/default will likely deter foreign direct [investment] inflows, restrict new [debt] issuance
and encourage capital flight," Jefferies fixed-income strategist Siobhan Morden said in a note Wednesday.

Terror Group's Riches: The Islamic State's Economy of Extortion
Terror Group's Riches: The Islamic State's Economy of Extortion
By Nour Malas and Maria Abi-Habib
1,629 words
28 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Islamic State runs a self-sustaining economy across territory it controls in Syria and Iraq, pirating oil while
exacting tribute from a population of at least eight million, Arab and Western officials said, making it one of
the world's richest terror groups and an unprecedented threat.
That illicit economy presents a new picture of Islamic State's financial underpinnings. The group was once
thought to depend on funding from Arab Gulf donors and donations from the broader Muslim world. Now,
Islamic State -- the former branch of al Qaeda that has swallowed parts of Iraq and Syria -- is a largely
self-financed organization.
Money from outside donors "pales in comparison to their self-funding through criminal and terrorist activities,"
a U.S. State Department official said, adding that those activities generate millions of dollars each month.
For Western and Arab nations that are striving to stop Islamic State, the group's local funding sources pose a
conundrum: A clampdown on economic activity that helps fund the group, counterterrorism officials and
experts said, could cause a humanitarian crisis in the already stressed areas Islamic State controls.
"Can you prevent ISIS from taking assets? Not really, because they're sitting on a lot of assets already," said
a Western counterterrorism official. "So you must disrupt the network of trade. But if you disrupt trade in
commodities like food, for example, then you risk starving thousands of civilians."
From Raqqa in Syria to Mosul in Iraq, Sunni radicals from the group administer an orderly extortion system of
business and farm tributes, public-transport fees and protection payments from Christians and other religious
minorities who choose to live under the militants rather than flee, according to residents of these areas,
analysts who have studied the group and government officials tracking it.
Islamic State also does business with people from some of the same regions whose governments are trying
to stamp it out. From the territory the group has taken, it controls the sale of oil, wheat and antiquities,
spurring a vast gray market with buyers as unlikely as the Syrian regime and Shiite and Kurdish businessmen
from Lebanon and Iraq, said Western officials and Syrians and Iraqis with knowledge of the now-common
business transactions.
"They have a stable economy, more or less, across their territory in Syria and Iraq," said Hasan Abu Hanieh,
a Jordanian scholar of Sunni radicalism who is an expert on al Qaeda and Islamic State.
Ransom payments are a major source of income for the group but are far less consistent than income from
domestic activities, Western officials said.
Al Qaeda in Iraq, formed by Jordanian national Abu Musab al-Zarqawi, was the backbone of the group that
expanded into Syria in the chaos of civil war, calling itself first ISIS or ISIL, Islamic State of Iraq and al-Sham
-- a historic geography spanning the Levant.
In June, after seizing Mosul, it dropped the geographic reference and declared an Islamic caliphate with
ambitions for broader territory, naming Iraqi national Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi its caliph. The group now calls
itself Islamic State, claiming to command western Iraq and northeastern Syria.
In its moves in Syria last year and its blitz through Iraq this year -- militants grabbed oil fields, crop lands and
central-bank branches -- Islamic State appeared to surprise outside observers. But Mideast experts say the
group remains very much the successor of al Qaeda in Iraq, which became al Qaeda's richest branch using
the same sorts of local taxation and extortion.
"At the time, no one could do any simple daily transaction or business -- a truck couldn't pass down the road
-- without payment," Mr. Abu Hanieh said of al Qaeda in Iraq's heyday. "This local revenue strategy

--- Page 130 ---
Page 130 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
continues," he said. Islamic State is also structurally similar to its predecessor and is believed to have
retained a financial committee with a "minister of finance" who oversees financial affairs, he said.
Interviews with Syrians and Iraqis with relatives living in Islamic State-controlled towns offered a snapshot of
how institutionalized the group's domestic fundraising and illegal trade has become -- and how difficult it may
be to rein in.
At least eight million Syrians and Iraqis live under full or partial Islamic State control, according to estimates
by the Syrian opposition and local Iraqi officials.
Earlier this year, as Islamic State militants swarmed northern Syria, one farming family got a knock on the
door that would strip them of their livelihoods.
The militants, who introduced themselves as Islamic State members, said they were administering the town
and had a list of how many acres of land and other assets the family owned, said one family member. They
also said they had a list of the town's Christian families and the tribute they had to pay to be able to stay living
there.
"They demanded to be paid in gold, silver or any other precious material for the annual crop we were about
to plant," said the family member, who has since fled. He said farmers were forced to pay tribute based on
land in their possession rather than the success of their crop -- a hardship, coming in drought year.
Western and Arab officials said there is little quantifiable detail about the domestic activity that finances the
group, because it varies between areas and because governments lack the intelligence capabilities to track
such information.
The proportion contributed by local fees and racketeering -- versus, for example, oil and agriculture sales -- is
also hard to gauge because smuggling and trading networks within Syria, Iraq and neighboring countries
have become so vast.
In Syria, the insurgents control eight oil and gas fields in the provinces of Raqqa and Deir Ezzor, according to
Syrian rebels who once controlled the fields.
Again, from these fields, they trade with people from the very countries that are fighting them. They sell heavy
oil at an average of $26 to $35 a barrel to local merchants, to merchants across the border in Iraq, or to
upstart refineries financed by Turkish, Lebanese and Iraqi businessmen, said Syrians and Lebanese involved
in the oil trade.
Light crude, which is higher priced on global markets, sells at up to $60 a barrel, these people said. Crude
smuggled out, rather than sold locally, is priced higher to account for the often complicated way out. Syrian
rebels estimated Islamic State-controlled fields produce between 30,000 and 70,000 barrels a day.
Trade beyond Syrian and Iraqi borders is buoyed by a network of business people. Kurdish merchants
transport oil into Iraqi Kurdistan and sell it either to Turkish or Iranian traders. Those traders smuggle it into
their countries and sell it at a discount over local prices, or sell it back to the Syrian government, said Syrians
and Iraqis involved in the oil trade.
Turkey's foreign ministry said the amount of oil authorities have seized along the Turkey-Syria border has
surged by 300% since the start of the Syrian uprising in 2011. "We are trying to see how we can stop this," a
senior Turkish foreign ministry official said, "but the border is very difficult to police."
Western officials said it has also become clearer, through recent kidnappings, that the group is methodically
using hostages to raise revenue. Given the small number of detainees released, they say, that revenue is
likely much less reliable than that from the local economy.
The other source of external funding, wealthy donors and radical Sunni individuals in the Gulf, has sharply
fallen as their governments recognize the severity of the Islamic State threat to the region, they said.
Earlier this month, the United Nations placed on a sanctions list six citizens it said were involved in funding or
recruiting for Islamic State and Nusra Front -- al Qaeda's official Syrian arm -- imposing a travel ban and
freezing their assets. Kuwait arrested one of its citizens on the list as he returned from Qatar last week, as
well as at least two other financiers, Kuwaiti officials said.
Al Qaeda disavowed Islamic State this year, citing its brutality, but Nusra Front remains on U.S. and
international blacklists.
The U.N. sanctions were the first international effort to stem the groups' revenues and operations. But
counterterrorism experts said that, because so much of the revenue is domestic and other trade is done in a
cash-only region, there are few effective ways to clamp down.

--- Page 131 ---
Page 131 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The U.N. assigned a committee to find ways to implement the effort and make recommendations by
November.
External donations are now "bonus payments" for Islamic State, said Douglas Ollivant, a former Iraq director
at the U.S. National Security Council and fellow with the New America Foundation, a think tank.
"They are remarkably creative in their financing efforts and they can operate independently of outside
sources," Mr. Ollivant said. And because "the whole region is a cash economy, it's very difficult to cut off
financing."
A senior Western diplomat said the U.N. sanctions effort was a positive, but rhetorical, step: "We are
pretending it's important to make it more tangible, but there is little real assessment of what we can do."
Alexander Evans, who leads the U.N. team investigating Islamic State funding, said oil trading, kidnapping
and assets seized in Iraq and Syria will continue to drive the group's income.
Measures against Islamic State's economic machine must weigh impact on the local populations, Mr. Evans
added: "Any new sanctions measures should be balanced between the need to disrupt ISIL funding and
maintaining the humanitarian needs of the people suffering under ISIL control."

Mohammad Nour al Akraa and Joe Parkinson contributed to this article.

S&P 500 Pushes to Another Record --- Index Climbs Above 2000 Milestone, But Can't Hold On; 'Less
News Is Good'
By Dan Strumpf
1,000 words
26 August 2014

C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The S&P 500 index pushed above 2000 for the first time, but failed to hold on through the close of trading, as
it managed to finish at a record for the 29th time this year.
The latest flirtation with a milestone for the five-year bull market rally comes as investor sentiment remains
buoyed by relatively friendly Federal Reserve policy, moderate growth in the U.S. economy and healthy
corporate profits.
The S&P 500 moved higher from the opening bell on Monday, breaking the 2000 mark at midmorning and
trading as high as 2001.95. However, stocks were unable to build a head of steam and the index faded during
afternoon trading. As is often the case with market milestones, the index didn't finish above the mark on its
first try.
For the day, the S&P 500 rose 9.52 points, or 0.5%, to 1997.92, up 8.1% on the year. In 2013, the S&P 500
closed at an all-time high 45 times.
"We would consider today's market to be strong nevertheless, kissing 2000 and then backing off," said Jim
Russell, senior equity strategist at U.S. Bank Wealth Management. "Our guess is that we take several
attempts at crossing that line and then we'll trade meaningfully above it,"
The advance Monday came on an otherwise quiet summer day for the stock market.
After a sudden swoon beginning in mid-July, sparked by geopolitical concerns about Ukraine and the
Mideast, stocks have been grinding slowly but steadily higher. The S&P is up 4.6% since its most recent low
hit on Aug. 7.
"Less news is good for this market," said Jerry Braakman, chief investment officer at First American Trust,
which manages about $1.1 billion. "Underlying earnings are strong. The economic [data] continue to
improve."
An absence of market-moving news left investors to focus on last week's remarks by top central bankers in
Jackson Hole, Wyo., including an address by Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen suggesting that any
interest-rate increase is still a way off.
Investors also remained focused on comments from European Central Bank President Mario Draghi, who
suggested a willingness for more aggressive stimulus from the ECB. European markets climbed in the wake
of his remarks Friday.
Other benchmarks also made strides on Monday. The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 75.65 points, or
0.4%, to 17076.87, 0.4% shy of its all-time high reached July 16. The Nasdaq Composite Index tacked on
18.80 points, or 0.4%, to 4557.35, 9.7% short of its dot-com-era record in 2000.
The move toward 2000 for the S&P 500 has been 16 years in the making since the index first crossed the
1000 mark in February 1998. But the push comes as many investors feel the bull market is facing a much
tougher slog than last year, when the S&P 500 jumped 30%.
Even though the economy is seen as being on a fairly stable growth path, and interest rates are expected to
remain low, some investors are nervous about the potential for the Fed to raise rates sooner than expected,
as well as higher-than-average share-price valuations that have made bargains much harder to find.
The market's advance has made investors like Mr. Braakman more conscious of the prices they pay for
individual stocks. Mr. Braakman said he is seeking out companies that have growth potential with an eye on

--- Page 133 ---

their price. He has been buying shares in the biotechnology, financial and energy sectors, such as Halliburton
and Exxon Mobil.

Ecuador's Phony Bitcoin Ploy
Americas
Ecuador's Phony Bitcoin Ploy
By Mary Anastasia O'Grady
859 words
25 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Few economic injustices are more villainous than stealing from the poor. Yet this is what a government does
when it devalues its currency. Pope Francis has stressed the Christian obligation to share with the least of
our brothers. Devaluation actually takes from them what they have earned themselves. And while the
inflation tax that follows hits everybody, it wallops the low-income population the hardest.
Ecuador engaged in this insidious practice repeatedly throughout most of the 20th century. The little nation
took a giant step in 2000 when it adopted the U.S. dollar as its currency to put an end to theft-by-devaluation.
But last month President Rafael Correa pushed through Congress a law to permit the creation of what
Ecuador calls a new "electronic" currency. So now the central bank again has a vehicle that will allow it to
conjure money out of thin air to finance a political agenda.
Ecuadoreans are not free to speak against this threat to their earnings and savings. Mr. Correa is well known
for using the judicial system and the army to threaten and silence his critics. Earlier this month he won the
passage of a new law that makes it a crime -- punishable by up to seven years in prison -- to "publish,
broadcast or spread" news that creates "economic panic." The law suggests that the brash University of
Illinois-trained economist is well aware of the trouble his funny money is likely to cause.
Venezuela's Nicolas Maduro and Argentina's Cristina Kirchner, both fellow travelers of the Ecuadorean
president, have driven their economies into the ground. Compared with them, Ecuador's dollarized economy
looks better. Seven years after Mr. Correa took power, Ecuador reports an average annual gross domestic
product growth rate of 4.3% and a drop in the poverty rate to 26% from 38%.
Still, economic statistics compiled by an authoritarian state should always be viewed with some skepticism,
as should a June poll that purportedly found the president has a 61% approval rating. Even the government's
numbers are hardly miraculous. According to development economists, it takes GDP growth of better than
6% for a decade to authentically move people out of poverty. Dollarization has made people better off by
protecting their savings and earnings, but Ecuador has achieved no progress in economic competitiveness,
the linchpin of growth. Instead Mr. Correa has run the country on the fumes of high-price oil and borrowing.
In 2008 Ecuador repudiated $3.9 billion in foreign debt. The government had the money to pay its creditors,
but the president had other plans for those funds. So Mr. Correa justified the default by alleging that the debt,
incurred by previous governments, was "illegitimate" and that bondholders were "real monsters." Then he
went on his own spending tear.
This year Ecuador will run a fiscal deficit, including debt service, of some $9.2 billion, more than 9% of GDP.
That's what happens with budgeting that forecasts that oil prices will grow to the sky. It will be hard to shrink
bloated state payrolls and subsidies, and the cost of servicing rising debt levels isn't getting any cheaper.
To return this year to the international capital markets with a $2 billion 10-year bond, Ecuador had to pay a
whopping 7.95% coupon. It also took out a $400 million three-year loan from Goldman Sachs against
Ecuadorean gold to meet budget shortfalls. China holds $11 billion in Ecuadorean debt, not including billions
of dollars in loans from Beijing secured by future oil shipments at an undisclosed price.
Now Mr. Correa is planning for when he runs out of other people's money. The central bank says its new
money will be a parallel currency backed up by dollars or the "equivalent" and used to pay its 500,000
bureaucrats in a "hygienic" manner. But if so, why not use dollars? In today's world, there's nothing special
about transferring money electronically. Implying that this is a "virtual" currency is an attempt to lend
Bitcoin-like cachet to what will essentially be IOUs issued by a country with a rather dodgy credit history.
Coming from a president who ran for office in 2006 pledging to de-dollarize an economy renowned for bouts
of hyperinflation, this is more than disconcerting.

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Pope Francis is not oblivious to the consequences of monetary meddling. In his November 2013 "apostolic
exhortation," he wrote: "Debt and the accumulation of interest also make it difficult for countries to realize the
potential of their own economies and keep citizens from enjoying their real purchasing power."
That's an excellent if somewhat cryptic summation of what may be the most ubiquitous cause of persistent
poverty in the world: government fiscal profligacy and the monetary devaluation that follows it. The pope's
statement goes to the heart of why Mr. Correa is building the escape hatch to get out of dollarization, and
what will happen to Ecuadoreans when he uses it.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Ecuador's Electronic Cash Is Strong" -- WSJ Sep 2, 2014)

Interest-Rate Views Trample Gold --- Reports Showing Healthy Economic Recovery Have Refocused Traders' Attention on Fed's Plans
Commodities Report
Interest-Rate Views Trample Gold --- Reports Showing Healthy Economic Recovery Have Refocused
Traders' Attention on Fed's Plans
By Tatyana Shumsky and Ira Iosebashvili
795 words
22 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold is losing its allure as investors brace for higher interest rates.
Prices of the precious metal have fallen 4.9% from their recent high on July 10, as a string of upbeat U.S.
economic reports has bolstered the case for tighter U.S. monetary policy. On Thursday, gold posted its
steepest decline in over a month as investors left the market ahead of Friday's speech by Federal Reserve
Chairwoman Janet Yellen at a gathering of central bankers in Jackson Hole, Wyo.
Gold catapulted to a record in 2011 as investors wagered that the Fed's unconventional efforts to boost
growth, including bond purchases and near-zero interest rates, would trigger higher inflation and weaken
the dollar. But the metal's price tumbled as inflation remained tame and the economy improved.
Now, Fed officials are debating when to raise interest rates, a move that would further diminish the case for
owning gold. Gold produces no income and struggles to compete with interest-bearing investments such as
Treasury bonds and bank deposits, whose yields will rise once market interest rates turn up. At the same
time, signs that crises in Ukraine and the Middle East are having a limited impact on global growth also have
reduced demand for gold as a haven.
Most investors expect a rate increase in mid-2015 at the earliest. However, some Fed officials argued for
accelerating that timetable during their July policy meeting, according to minutes released Wednesday.
On Thursday, gold for August delivery, the front-month contract, dropped $19.70, or 1.5%, to $1,273.70 a
troy ounce, a two-month low.
"Gold's longer-term fundamentals are firmly aligned toward the bearish," said Paul Christopher, a St.
Louis-based chief international strategist at Wells Fargo Advisors, which manages $1.4 trillion.
Investors who believe in a strengthening economy are faced with a future "where yields are higher, and there
is no inflation in sight," Mr. Christopher said. "Historically, that's been bad for gold." Mr. Christopher has
been advising clients to sell gold on rallies, believing prices will drift lower.
Even after the recent pullback, gold prices remain up 6% this year. Violence in Ukraine and Iraq have
bolstered demand from traders who view gold as holding its value better than other assets during periods of
political or economic turmoil. Not all U.S. economic data have been positive, leading some to question the
timing of the Fed's first interest-rate increase.
On Thursday, labor, manufacturing and housing indicators came in above expectations. Combined, the data
point to a healthy recovery and have refocused gold traders' attention on interest rates, analysts say.
The odds of a June 2015 interest-rate increase rose to 46% on Thursday, a day after the Fed's meeting
minutes were released, from 40% on Tuesday, according to data from CME Group Inc. based on the 30-day
federal-funds futures. The dollar, which becomes more attractive to hold when interest rates rise, also has
risen to multimonth highs against the euro and yen.
"We do expect rates to rise and the dollar to strengthen, and that's not a conducive background for gold,"
said Mohan Mandrekar, an analyst at Bessemer Trust, which has $1.2 billion invested in commodities and
has been underweight precious metals relative to the Dow JonesUBS Commodities Index since March.
Nicholas Johnson, co-portfolio manager at the $25 billion Pimco CommodityPlus Strategy Fund, said gold's
recent pullback makes it cheap relative to other haven assets, giving the metal an allure should violence
intensify in the Middle East or Ukraine.

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"If you have an increase in the geopolitical concerns or a decline in the equity markets, gold should do really
well because it's a flight-to-quality asset and it's really cheap," he said. Mr. Johnson holds bullish gold
futures and has been selling put options, which act like an insurance policy against lower prices. His options
bet that gold won't fall below $1,250 to $1,260 a troy ounce, where the options would pay out.
But other investors say a rebounding economy spells trouble for gold. In addition to the threat of tighter
monetary policy, a record-breaking rally in U.S. equities has siphoned money away from the gold market,
while data pointing to tame consumer price increases has damped interest in gold as a store of value.
"The belief is that we're on the road to recovery, rates are heading higher, and gold is a little richer than it
should be," said Thomas Capalbo, a broker at Newedge.

European Markets: Investors Raise Their Shields Against Russia --- As Conflict in Ukraine Continues, Fund Managers Are Bracing for...
European Markets: Investors Raise Their Shields Against Russia --- As Conflict in Ukraine Continues,
Fund Managers Are Bracing for Protracted Instability, Rattling Already-Fragile Markets
By Ira Iosebashvili
891 words
19 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors are scrambling to reduce their exposure to the potential economic fallout from continuing fighting in
Ukraine, unsettling already-fragile markets.
Currencies in Central and Eastern Europe, including the Hungarian forint and Polish zloty, have plunged to at
least one-year lows against the dollar as investors pull back from the economies most vulnerable to blowback
from sanctions against Russia. European stocks also have sagged.
Investors are responding to escalating violence and tit-for-tat sanctions that are reverberating throughout the
region's financial markets and exposing new vulnerabilities in Europe's struggling economy. European
nations and the U.S. imposed broad sanctions on Russian individuals and banks in late July, alleging the
country is supporting rebels in eastern Ukraine. Russia retaliated by barring food imports from countries that
backed the sanctions.
On Friday, oil prices jumped, U.S. stocks fell and gold erased earlier losses after Ukraine's president said the
country's military had destroyed an armored Russian column. Those markets snapped back on Monday after
Western and Ukrainian officials played down the attack, while Russia denied it occurred. The moves
underscored how sensitive markets have become to flaring tensions in the region.
The escalating conflict between Russia and the West adds another layer of uncertainty to markets already
grappling with uneven economic growth and a shift in policy at the Federal Reserve. As markets grow
choppier, investors are being pushed to adjust their portfolios to a more uncertain time. So far, the drop in
asset prices hasn't attracted bargain hunters, a sign that many investors see more trouble ahead.
"The European recovery was pretty fragile to begin with, and now you have this instability on its border," said
David Chalupnik, who oversees the $8.5 billion equity portfolio at Nuveen Asset Management.
Mr. Chalupnik said his fund had bet a year ago that a recovery in the U.S. and Europe would lift shares of
industrial companies. But tensions between Russian and the West worsened and the sector turned in weak
second-quarter earnings. In the past month, the fund has sold positions in companies it believed had high
exposure to Europe, including manufacturer Eaton Corp.
"We thought it was a good time to get out," Mr. Chalupnik said.
Eastern European countries, which send a larger share of their exports to Russia, have been among the
hardest hit by the investor exodus. Overall, equity and bond investments into emerging European economies
have declined sharply in August. Retail stock investors have pulled $234 million out of the region this month
through Aug. 11, after investing $33 million last month and $891 million in June, according to EPFR Global.
The Polish zloty is down 3% since July 1, while the Hungarian forint is off 4% and the Czech Koruna has lost
4%.
Others are buying assets they expect will rise if the conflict with Russia worsens. Russia is one of the world's
biggest producers of palladium, nickel and aluminum, and the second-biggest oil exporter. So far sanctions
haven't barred commodities exports. But some investors worry that commodities could be affected by future
restrictions, or that Russia could clamp down on exports in retaliation.
Palladium futures, a market that is particularly sensitive to developments involving Russia, hit a 13-year high
on Monday, settling at $894.90 a troy ounce.
Huntington Asset Advisors, a $1.8 billion fund based in Cincinnati, recently has taken positions in miners
including Rio Tinto PLC, Southern Copper Corp. and Vale, betting that metal prices will rise if commodity-rich
Russia clamped down on exports of metals such as nickel or aluminum. All three stocks are up by 4% or
more since July 1.

--- Page 142 ---
Page 142 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Basically, we wanted to buy these companies as an insurance policy," said Peter Sorrentino, a portfolio
manager at the fund.
To be sure, most investors believe the military conflict itself will stay confined to Ukraine and not spread to
other countries. In their present form, the sanctions are expected to affect only a small percentage of
Europe's production, and the European Central Bank's willingness to stimulate the economy is likely to
ensure that any damage to the EU is limited, investors said.
"There could be an impact, but given what we know now, that impact will be temporary and small," said Dan
Loewy, chief investment officer and co-head of multi-asset solutions at AllianceBernstein, which oversees
more than $100 billion. Mr. Loewy says his fund hasn't made any changes in its investments to account for
Russia-related risk, other than to hedge some areas of the portfolio with put options, which would rise in value
as other investments declined.
Still, some investors are doing what they can to avoid being caught by surprise.
Jack Ablin, chief investment officer at BMO Private Bank, with $66 billion under management, said his firm on
Thursday decided to cut half of its position in Libor-backed bank loans, a relatively illiquid asset that could fall
sharply during a sudden escalation in geopolitical risk, and buy short-term corporate bonds.
The Ukraine situation is "a gateway that can turn geopolitical confrontations into economic problems," he
said. "We just needed some dry gunpowder."

Markets Roiled by Ukraine Conflict --- Calm Spell for Global Stocks Is Broken; Treasury-Bond Yields Drop and Crude-Oil Price Jumps
Friday's Markets
Markets Roiled by Ukraine Conflict --- Calm Spell for Global Stocks Is Broken; Treasury-Bond Yields
Drop and Crude-Oil Price Jumps
By Alexandra Scaggs and Min Zeng

The Wall Street Journal

Fresh conflict in Ukraine jolted global markets, igniting selling in stocks and another round of investor flight to
haven government bonds.
The news broke a recent calm spell in the markets, where stocks had stabilized after being hit by geopolitical
concerns starting in late July.
U.S. blue-chip stocks fell, as did European stocks, after news that Ukrainian artillery destroyed a large part of
a column of armored vehicles that had been seen entering the country from Russia. The turn of events was
seen as potentially escalating a situation that many had thought might be easing.
"The encounter in Ukraine was a hefty reminder that geopolitics cannot be ignored," said CMC Markets
analyst Jasper Lawler.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average sank as many as 87 points within 30 minutes of the news, falling 0.9%
from its session high. It extended that decline in afternoon trading, then later recouped some of those losses
to close down 50.67 points, or 0.3%, to 16662.91.
Meanwhile, investors flocked into U.S. Treasury bonds, pushing yields lower, and crude-oil prices made their
sharpest single-session advance in a month.
Major stock benchmarks in the U.S. recouped the worst of their losses in afternoon trading, to close in mixed
territory. At the Dow industrials' session low, the index was down as many as 138 points, a 1.2% dip from its
morning highs.
European stocks took the brunt of the selling. Germany's DAX index saw the worst declines, shedding 1.4%
as investors fled to the safety of government bonds. The 10-year German government bond's yield settled at
0.967%, the first time on record that the yield closed below 1%, according to Tradeweb.
Stock-trading volumes were relatively light, which led benchmarks to make relatively sharp moves at midday,
traders said. Shares changed hands at a faster pace than they have this month, on average, but stock-trading
volume remained below the average for this year.
"We're all paying attention to what's going on" in Ukraine, said Keith Bliss, senior vice president at brokerage
Cuttone & Co. And "you're going to have an outsize reaction, because of the thin volume."
The S&P 500 shed 0.12 point, less than 0.1%, to 1955.06. The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 11.93 points,
or 0.3%, to 4464.93.
Even with Friday's declines, U.S. stocks advanced for the week. The S&P 500 ended the week 1.2% higher,
and the Dow rose 0.7%. The Nasdaq closed with a 2.2% gain, its biggest single-week advance since late
May.
"The relative calm in the Russia-Ukraine crisis was disrupted," said Jeremy Zirin, chief U.S. equity strategist
for UBS Wealth Management Research. But "the main story over the past week and a half has been that U.S.
stocks are hanging in there."
Meanwhile, U.S. Treasury bonds continued a rally that has surprised many investors. The yield on the
10-year U.S. Treasury note fell, settling at 2.345%, its lowest since June 2013.

"It is flight to quality for everyone," said Thomas Roth, executive director in the U.S. government-bond trading

Gold Hurt by Stronger U.S. Economic Data
Commodities Report
Gold Hurt by Stronger U.S. Economic Data
By Tatyana Shumsky
557 words
16 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold prices fell, as signs of health in the U.S. economy outweighed news of a skirmish near
the Ukraine-Russia border.
Gold for December delivery, the most actively traded contract, dropped $9.50, or 0.7%, to settle at $1,306.20
a troy ounce Friday on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. This was the lowest
settlement since Aug. 5, when futures closed at $1,285.30 an ounce, and was down 0.4% on the week.
Prices fell to $1,293 an ounce in early trading amid signs of diplomatic progress in Eastern Europe, as
Ukrainian border guards and customs officers inspected a convoy of nearly 300 trucks that Russia said
carried humanitarian aid.
But gold pulled higher as news of a Russian incursion into Ukraine renewed concerns about stability in the
region. Ukrainian troops engaged a column of armored vehicles that crossed into the country from Russia,
destroying a large part of the military equipment, Ukrainian President Petro Poroshenko said. Russia has
denied sending men and weapons into eastern Ukraine.
There was "enough uncertainty for prices to rebound and stabilize going into the weekend," said Bill O'Neill, a
principal with commodities investment firm Logic Advisors in Upper Saddle River, N.J.
The fact that gold prices didn't fully recoup the earlier losses shows the complacency that many traders feel
in relation to the Ukrainian conflict, said Bob Haberkorn, a senior commodities broker with RJO Futures in
Chicago. "We've had continuous flare-ups between Ukraine and Russia for months now, and traders are
starting to see it as just another day," he said. "An all-out war isn't in anyone's interest at this point."
In addition, crises in the Middle East appeared to cool, which weighed on gold prices. Hamas extended its
cease-fire with Israel, while in northern Iraq, a U.S. assessment team concluded that the scale of the
humanitarian crisis appears to be smaller than initially thought.
While some traders view gold as a shield from geopolitical and financial turmoil, their interest in the haven
asset fades as their outlook brightens.
Still, the gold market remains under pressure as stronger U.S. economic data have stoked investor jitters
about higher interest rates from the Federal Reserve. Gold costs money to store and struggles to compete
with yield-bearing assets when interest rates are on the rise.
On Friday, data showed that U.S. industrial production increased more than expected in July while producer
prices, a measure of wholesale inflation, remained stable.
The Fed has indicated it would raise interest rates for the first time since 2006 once the economy appears to
be on firmer footing. Many investors and economists expect U.S. rates to rise sometime in 2015.
"The fact that gold couldn't get back to unchanged territory shows just how weak this market is right now,"
Mr. Haberkorn said.
In other precious-metals markets, palladium futures ended at a new 13-year high on concerns the
Ukraine-Russia conflict could disrupt supplies. Russia is the world's largest producer of palladium, widely
used to scrub emissions from gasoline-fueled engines. Palladium for September delivery rose $8.35, or 0.9%,
to $894.50 a troy ounce on Nymex.
